Clarifying al-Hayyiz and al-Khali
Commentary on the terms 'location' and 'void'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of 'al-hayyiz' (location) and 'al-khali' (void) in kalam, including how they relate to the indivisible atom and hyle.

Core Idea
Location is broader than place; void is empty of any occupant.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies two key terms from the previous discussion: 'al-hayyiz' (location) and 'al-khali' (void). He explains why the author specifies 'place' when referring to 'location' to avoid a common objection about the indivisible atom. He also defines 'void' by distinguishing it from what occupies space, using the analogy of hyle and forms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Notice the technical terms.
2
Understand the objection about the atom
See how specifying 'place' (makan) prevents the objection that location would apply to the indivisible atom. The atom is 'mutahayyiz' but not 'mutamakkin'.
3
Grasp the definition of 'void'
The phrase 'void of what occupies space' means the void itself is free from any occupier, similar to how hyle is void of forms.
4
Reflect on the distinctions
Compare 'mutamakkin' and 'mutahayyiz' and think about why these distinctions are important in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: في الحيز في المكان أشار بقوله: في المكان إلى أنه المراد بالحيز، فلا يرد على جعل الحيز كمالاً للجسم تحققه للجوهر الفرد، لأنه ليس بمتمكن وإن كان متحيزاً لوجود الامتداد في المتمكن كما صرحوا به. قوله: الخالي عن المتحيز أي في حد ذاته بمعنى أن المتحيز ليس مأخوذاً معه كما يقال للهيولى خال عن الصور في نفسها.
His saying: 'in the location (al-hayyiz) in place (al-makan)'—he indicated by his saying 'in place' that it is what is meant by location, so the objection is not made against making location a perfection for the body that it applies to the indivisible atom, because it is not occupying space (mutamakkin) even though it is occupying space (mutahayyiz) due to the existence of extension in the occupier, as they have explained. His saying: 'void of what occupies space' means in itself, i.e., that the occupier is not taken with it, as it is said that hyle is void of forms in itself.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage provides commentary on key terms from the previous discussion. The commentator explains that the author's phrase 'in the location in place' specifies that 'place' is what is meant by 'location'. This specification avoids a potential objection: if location were considered a perfection of the body, it would also apply to the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد). The response is that the indivisible atom is not 'occupying space' (متمكن) in the sense of being extended, even though it is 'occupying space' (متحيز) in a general sense because extension exists in the occupier. The commentator then clarifies the phrase 'void of what occupies space' (الخالي عن المتحيز). This phrase means that in itself, the space is considered free from the occupier, analogous to how hyle (هيولى) is said to be void of forms in itself.

Key Terms
الحيز
المكان
الجوهر الفرد
متمكن
متحيز
الهيولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The indivisible atom occupies both location and place in the same sense.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the atom is 'mutahayyiz' (occupies location) but not 'mutamakkin' (does not occupy place in the sense of having extension). Location is a broader concept than place.

Checkpoint
Why does the author specify 'in place' after 'location'?
A) To emphasize that location and place are synonyms.
B) To avoid an objection about the indivisible atom.
C) To explain that the indivisible atom is not located.
D) To show that location is not a perfection.

Key Takeaway
The terms 'location' (al-hayyiz) and 'void' (al-khali) have precise definitions that avoid logical objections and clarify the nature of substances like the indivisible atom and hyle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever treated two related concepts as identical (like 'location' and 'place') and later realized that this confusion led you to a wrong conclusion about Allah's attributes? How can you train yourself to ask, 'Is this term being used in a precise, technical sense?' before making judgments?
?
The void (al-khali) is defined as 'empty in itself'. When you reflect on the universe coming from nothing, does this concept help you appreciate that absolute non-existence cannot be the source of existence? How does the precision of these terms protect your understanding of tawheed?
?
If someone asked you, 'Is Allah in a place?' how would your answer change after distinguishing hayyiz from makan? Are you willing to struggle with these distinctions in your mind to avoid speaking about Allah without knowledge?

The Two Pronunciations of 'Awaj'
The commentator explains the linguistic nuances of 'awaj'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic rule distinguishing the pronunciation of 'awaj' (عوج) in Arabic, based on whether it refers to abstract or concrete crookedness.

Core Idea
The word 'awaj' shifts pronunciation depending on abstract or concrete meaning.

This passage examines a subtle linguistic rule governing the word 'awaj' (crookedness). The commentator explains that its pronunciation changes based on whether the crookedness is perceived by insight (abstract) or by sight (concrete), supported by Quranic usage and classical scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms: عَوَج and عِوَج.
The passage is from a commentary on a theological text, but here it is purely linguistic.
2
Identify the main rule
The commentator states that عوج is pronounced with fatḥa (عَوَج) for abstract intelligibles and with kasra (عِوَج) for concrete sensibles. Find this statement and note how it is supported by Quran 20:107.
Look for the phrase 'بفتح العين' and 'بالكسر'.
3
Examine the alternative view
The passage mentions an opposite view from some linguists and then cites Ibn al-Sikkit, who gives another rule based on the type of object (upright objects vs. land/religion/livelihood).
Ibn al-Sikkit's rule is different: عوج with fatḥa for upright things, with kasra for abstract or land matters.
4
Reflect on the precision of Arabic
This passage shows the subtlety of Arabic vocabulary. Even a single root can have nuanced pronunciations. Consider how this precision affects theological interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا) عوج فيه العوج العطف من حال الانتصاب، وهو بفتح العين فيما يدرك بالبصيرة والفكر من المعقولات وبالكسر فيما يدرك بالبصر من المحسوسات، هكذا وجدت بخط جدي في حواشي المطول ويؤيده قوله تعالى : ولا ترى فيها عوجاً ولا أمتا ﴾ [طه: ۱۰۷] ، وربما يقال : عكس هذا أيضاً حتى قال بعض أهل اللغة: العوج بالفتح كحبي در جوب و مانند آن وبالكسر كحبي دردين ،و مانند آن وقال ابن السكيت كل ما كان ينتصب كالحائط والعود قيل فيه عوج بالفتح والعوج بالكسر ما كان في أرض أو دين أو معاش.
His saying: 'no crookedness (عوج) in it' – al-awaj (العوج) is deviation from an upright state. It is with fatḥa on the 'ayn when it is perceived by insight and thought from intelligibles, and with kasra when perceived by sight from sensibles. Thus I found in the handwriting of my grandfather in the margins of al-Muṭawwal. And it is supported by His saying, exalted: 'And you will see in it no crookedness (عوج) nor any amt' [Quran 20:107]. And it may be said: this is also the opposite. Some linguists said: al-awaj with fatḥa is like 'حب در جوب' and the like, and with kasra is like 'حب در دين' and the like. And Ibn al-Sikkīt said: everything that stands upright like a wall or a stick, the word ʻawaj (with fatḥa) is said of it, and ʻiwaj (with kasra) is what is in land, religion, or livelihood.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic analysis of the word 'عوج' (crookedness) from the earlier phrase. The commentator explains that 'عوج' is deviation from an upright state. He then distinguishes its pronunciation: with a fatḥa (عَوَج) when referring to abstract intelligibles perceived by insight, and with a kasra (عِوَج) when referring to concrete sensibles perceived by sight. He confirms this from his grandfather's marginal notes on al-Muṭawwal and supports it with Quran 20:107. He notes that some linguists reverse this, and then cites Ibn al-Sikkīt for a different rule: upright objects like walls take عَوَج (fatḥa), while matters of land, religion, or livelihood take عِوَج (kasra).

Key Terms
العوج
العطف
الانتصاب
الفتح
الكسر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'عوج' always has the same pronunciation regardless of context.

Defending the Elative Construction
Defense of the elative construction against grammatical objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars defend the grammatical validity of the elative construction 'أجل من أن يباهي' against objections regarding the object of comparison.

Core Idea
Grammatical objections to theological phrases can be resolved through implied verbs or contextual elision.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a grammatical objection to the phrase 'أجل من أن يباهي' (greater than that He should boast). The objection is that the noun after 'min' does not share the root verb, making it an invalid comparison. The commentator defends the construction by showing that the missing elements can be implied. This lesson breaks down that defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the structure: objection, first response, second response, final observation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection
Find the grammatical issue raised against 'ajal min an yubāhī'. Why is the object after 'min' considered unsuitable?
The objection is that the term after 'min' does not share the root verb (e.g., jalālah) with the elative.
3
Understand the first response
The commentator says 'min' is attached to an implied verb within the elative. What is that implied verb? How does it fix the comparison?
The elative implies a verb like 'muta'ābid' meaning 'far in majesty'.
4
Understand the second response
Alternatively, the comparative 'min' can be omitted due to context. The example from Quran 20:7 illustrates this. How does that work?
The word after 'min' becomes the object of comparison directly.
5
Reflect on the final observation
The commentator notes that a clearer wording would be 'than whoever can', but the author chose a descriptive form. Why might that be acceptable?
This shows sensitivity to both grammar and intended meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والمعنى أنه أجل من متعلق (المباهاة المقصود من هذا التكلف دفع ما يورد على التركيب المذكور وأمثاله من أن ما بعد من لا يصلح أن يكون مفضلاً عليه، إذ ليس يشارك ما قبلها في أصل الفعل أعني الجلالة مثلاً، لم يلتفت إلى ما يقال من أن من متعلقة بفعل يتضمنه : اسم التفضيل أي متباعد في الجلالة من أن يباهي تحرزاً عن لزوم استعمال أفعل التفضيل حينئذ بدون الأشياء الثلاثة كما صرح به في شرحه للمفتاح وإن أمكن أن يجاب بأن من التفضيلية محذوفة بقرينة المقام، كما في قوله تعالى : فإنه يعلم السر وأخفى ﴾ [طه: ٧]، والمعنى هو أجل من سائر الملوك ، ثم الظاهر في العبارة أن يقال : ممن يمكن لكنه أراد الوصف أي من ملك يمكن أن تتعلق به المباهاة فأورد على ما ذكر وأمثاله ما آت سورة الكافرين وغيرها .
His saying: (And the meaning is that he is greater than the object of boasting) – the intent of this artifice is to rebut what is raised against the mentioned construction and its likes: that the term after 'min' is not suitable to be the object of comparison, since it does not share with the former in the root verb, i.e., majesty for example. He did not pay attention to what is said: that 'min' is attached to a verb implied by the elative name, i.e., 'far in majesty from that he should boast,' guarding against the necessity of using the elative without the three conditions, as he stated in his commentary on al-Miftāḥ. And if it can be answered that the comparative 'min' is omitted due to contextual indication, as in His saying, exalted: 'Indeed, He knows the secret and what is even more hidden' [Quran 20:7], and the meaning is 'he is greater than all other kings.' Then apparently the expression should say: 'than whoever can' but he intended the description, i.e., 'than a king to whom boasting can be attached.' So he raised against what was mentioned and its likes what brought Sura al-Kāfirūn and others.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical defense. The commentator explains that the phrase 'أجل من أن يباهي' (greater than that he should boast) is constructed with the elative (اسم التفضيل) followed by 'min' and a verbal clause. The objection is that the term after 'min' (أن يباهي) is not a valid object of comparison because it does not share the root verb (e.g., جلالة) with the elative. The commentator responds by saying that this difficulty can be resolved by taking 'min' as attached to a verb implied in the elative, meaning 'he is far in majesty from that he should boast.' Alternatively, one can say that the comparative 'min' is omitted and the verb after it is the object of comparison, like in Quran 20:7. Ultimately, the intended meaning is 'he is greater than all kings.' The commentator notes that a clearer wording would be 'than whoever can', but the author chose a descriptive form.

Key Terms
أجل من أن يباهي
اسم التفضيل
الأشياء الثلاثة
من التفضيلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The construction 'أجل من أن يباهي' is grammatically incorrect because the noun after 'min' does not share the root verb.
Correction: The construction is valid either by implying a verb within the elative or by omitting the comparative 'min' due to context.

Checkpoint
What grammatical issue does the comment address?
A) The noun after 'min' does not share the same verb
B) The elative lacks the comparative meaning
C) The word 'yubāhī' is misspelled
D) The construction is too long

Key Takeaway
The elative construction 'أجل من أن يباهي' is grammatically valid because the object of comparison can be implied or the comparative 'min' omitted by context.

The Three Virtues of the Soul: Wisdom, Chastity, and Courage
Author defines the three virtues of the soul and clarifies the term 'wisdom'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the three foundational virtues of the soul as moderation in specific powers, and how their combination constitutes justice.

Core Idea
Virtue is moderation: wisdom in livelihood, chastity in desire, courage in anger.

This passage introduces the three cardinal virtues of the soul according to Islamic ethical theory. The author defines each virtue as a form of moderation relative to distinct inner powers, and notes that together they produce justice. He also warns against confusing this 'wisdom' with its philosophical counterpart, ensuring we use the term precisely in this context.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms: الحكمة, العفة, الشجاعة, العدالة, التوسط.
The passage is concise; each term carries a specific meaning.
2
Identify each virtue's definition
The passage defines wisdom and chastity as 'moderation in managing livelihood' and 'moderation with respect to the appetitive power'. Courage is 'moderation with respect to the irascible power'. Write these definitions in your own words.
Moderation (tawassuṭ) is the core concept.
3
Understand the combination into justice
Note that the combination of the three virtues is called justice (al-ʿadāla). Reflect on how balancing these three aspects leads to a just soul.
4
Avoid the common confusion about wisdom
The commentator clarifies that this wisdom is not theoretical wisdom. Consider why this distinction matters: this wisdom is practical, dealing with livelihood, not abstract knowledge.
This prevents a misunderstanding that could mix ethical virtues with philosophical categories.

قوله : (أصولها ثلاثة الحكمة والعفة والشجاعة الحكمة والعفة هي هي التوسط في تدبير المعاش، التوسط بالنسبة إلى القوة الشهوانية والشجاعة هى التوسط بالنسبة إلى القوة الغضبية و مجمع الثلاثة العدالة وسيفصل الشارح هذه المعاني في أواخر مباحث الكيفيات النفسانية، وتحقق أن هناك الحكمة المذكورة ها هنا ليست هي الحكمة التي جعلت قسيمة للحكمة النظرية، كما توهم ولا الحكمة التي قسمت إلى العملية والنظرية.
His saying: (Their foundations are three: wisdom (ḥikma), chastity (ʿiffa), and courage (shajāʿa). Wisdom and chastity are – they are – moderation (tawassuṭ) in managing livelihood, moderation with respect to the appetitive power (al-quwwa al-shahwāniyya), and courage is moderation with respect to the irascible power (al-quwwa al-ghaḍabiyya). Commentary
This passage defines the three virtues that are the foundations of the soul's excellence. Wisdom (ḥikma) and chastity (ʿiffa) are both identified with moderation (tawassuṭ): wisdom is moderation in managing livelihood, and chastity is moderation with respect to the appetitive power (desire). Courage (shajāʿa) is moderation with respect to the irascible power (anger). The combination of these three virtues results in justice (al-ʿadāla). The commentator also warns against confusion: the wisdom mentioned here is not the same as theoretical wisdom (al-ḥikma al-naẓariyya) nor the wisdom that is divided into practical and theoretical. This distinction prevents a common misunderstanding.

The Three Cardinal Virtues of the Soul: Wisdom, Chastity, and Courage
Author defines the three cardinal virtues as forms of moderation.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
The three virtues are not opposites but golden means between extremes.

This passage from a kalam text defines the foundational virtues that shape moral character. The author presents wisdom, chastity, and courage not as abstract ideals but as specific forms of moderation (tawassut) in managing our inner faculties. He also clarifies that the 'wisdom' here is a practical virtue, not the theoretical wisdom of philosophy. This lesson unpacks each term and its relationship to justice.

Second Shubha on Defining Knowledge
Sanusi presents and resolves the second shubha on defining knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to the definition of absolute knowledge (circularity between conceptualization and occurrence) and how al-Sanusi resolves it by distinguishing two modes of imprinting.

Core Idea
Conceptualizing knowledge does not require possessing it; they are different imprints.

This lesson tackles a subtle objection against the definition of absolute knowledge: Does conceptualizing knowledge require already having some knowledge, leading to circularity? Al-Sanusi presents the objection and then resolves it by distinguishing between two ways a quiddity can be imprinted in the soul. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the precise definition of knowledge in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: first the problem, then the resolution.
Pay attention to the terms 'tasawwur' and 'husul'.
2
Identify the circularity problem
The shubha claims that conceptualizing (tasawwur) the quiddity of knowledge depends on having particular knowledge of something else, which itself depends on conceptualization. This creates a vicious circle.
Ask yourself: why would this be a problem for defining knowledge?
3
Understand the two kinds of imprinting
Al-Sanusi distinguishes two ways the quiddity of knowledge can be imprinted (irtisam) in the soul: (1) by itself within its particulars (this is occurrence/husul, not conceptualization), and (2) by its example/mithal or form/sura (this is conceptualization/tasawwur, not occurrence).
The analogy with courage helps: having courage is different from conceptualizing courage.
4
See how the resolution works
The definition of knowledge requires the second kind of imprinting (conceptualization), not the first. Since conceptualization does not depend on occurrence, the circularity disappears.
Both shubhat (the first and second) are resolved by this distinction.
5
Connect to the overall definition
Reflect on how this resolution clarifies that absolute knowledge is defined as a conceptualization by example, not as an occurrence qualifying the soul.
This makes the definition precise and avoids circularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الشبهة الثانية تخيل أن تصور ماهية العلم إذا توقف على حصول علم جزئي متعلق بالغير، ولا شك أنه يتوقف على حصول ماهيته في ضمنه قائمة في النفس وهذا معنى تصورها فقد توقف كل منهما على الآخر، وإذا ظهر الفرق بينهما بأن ارتسام ماهية العلم في النفس على وجهين، أحدهما : أن ترتسم فيها بنفسها في ضمن جزئياتها، وذلك حصولها وليس تصورها ولا مستلزماً على قياس حصول الشجاعة للنفس الموجب لاتصافها بها، من غير أن تتصورها والثاني : أن ترتسم فيها بمثالها، وبصورتها وهذا هو تصورها لا حصولها على قياس تصور الشجاعة الذي لا يوجب اتصاف النفس بها، وهو المطلوب بتعريفها اضمحلت الشبهتان بالكلية .
And in the second shubha, he imagined that the conceptualization of the quiddity of knowledge, if it depends on the occurrence of a particular knowledge related to something else, and there is no doubt that it depends on the occurrence of its quiddity within that particular, subsisting in the soul, and this is the meaning of its conceptualization, then each depends on the other. But when the difference between them becomes clear—namely, that the imprinting of the quiddity of knowledge in the soul is of two kinds: one is that it is imprinted in it by itself within its particulars, and that is its occurrence, not its conceptualization, nor is it entailed by it, analogous to the occurrence of courage in the soul which necessitates its being described by it without conceptualizing it; and the second is that it is imprinted in it by its example and its form, and this is its conceptualization, not its occurrence, analogous to the conceptualization of courage which does not necessitate the soul being described by it—and this is what is sought by its definition, then both shubhat disappear entirely.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents and resolves the second shubha concerning the definition of absolute knowledge. It imagines a circularity: if the conceptualization (tasawwur) of the quiddity of knowledge depends on the occurrence (husul) of a particular knowledge of something else, and that occurrence itself depends on the conceptualization, then there is a loop. The resolution is by distinguishing two ways the quiddity of knowledge can be imprinted (irtisam) in the soul. The first is imprinting by itself within its particulars—this is mere occurrence, not conceptualization, and it does not entail conceptualization. It is like the occurrence of courage in the soul, which results in the soul being described as courageous without necessarily conceptualizing courage. The second is imprinting by an example (mithal) or form (sura) of the quiddity—this is conceptualization, not occurrence, and it does not entail the soul being described by that attribute. Analogously, conceptualizing courage does not make one courageous. The definition of absolute knowledge seeks this second kind of imprinting. With this distinction, both shubhat (the first and second) are completely resolved.

Key Terms
ماهية العلم
ارتسام
مثال
صورة
اتصاف
تصور
حصول
شجاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that conceptualization of a quiddity requires its occurrence in the soul in a way that qualifies the soul with that attribute.
Correction: Conceptualization is a separate imprint by example, not by the quiddity itself. It does not require the soul to possess the attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the difference between the two kinds of imprinting of the quiddity of knowledge in the soul according to the second shubha's resolution?
A) One is by itself within particulars (occurrence), the other by example/form (conceptualization).
B) One is necessary, the other is contingent.
C) One is innate, the other is acquired.
D) One is of knowledge, the other is of other things.

Key Takeaway
Conceptualizing a quiddity (like knowledge) does not require having that attribute; it is a separate imprint by example, not by the quiddity itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You can describe the attribute of patience (sabr) in detail, but do you actually embody it when tested? Which mode of imprinting is at work in your soul right now—an example of patience or the reality of patience? What will you do to move from conceptualization to occurrence?
?
The lesson says that conceptualizing courage does not make you courageous. Think of an Islamic virtue you 'know' about (e.g., humility, trust in Allah). Does your conceptualization of it convict you, or does it allow you to remain comfortably unchanged? When will you stop merely defining and start becoming?
?
If your knowledge of Allah's names and attributes is merely conceptual—an imprint by example—does that satisfy you? Or do you long for that knowledge to become a true occurrence in your soul, transforming how you live? What specific step will you take today to let one of Allah's attributes really settle in your heart?

Mutually Exclusive Concepts and Their Contradiction in Propositions
Explains how two concepts that exclude each other in themselves only become contradictory when considered in propositions about a subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between conceptual mutual exclusivity and propositional contradiction, and why the former does not entail the latter without a subject.

Core Idea
Concepts alone do not contradict; contradiction requires a proposition.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif unpacks a subtle but crucial point in logic and kalam: two concepts that are mutually exclusive in themselves, such as 'human' and 'non-human', do not actually contradict each other when considered simply as concepts. Contradiction only arises when these concepts are proposed as attributes of a subject, resulting in two contradictory propositions. This lesson will guide you through the author's reasoning and clarify common misinterpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Note the key terms that appear.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the main claim
The author states that mutual exclusion exists between concepts themselves, but not between conceptions. Find where this claim is made and how it is justified.
Look for the phrase 'لا تمانع بين التصورات'.
3
Understand the example
The example of human and non-human shows that they do not contradict as concepts, but only when considered as predicates for a subject. Reflect on why this is important for theological arguments.
4
Examine the variations in contradiction
The later part of the passage discusses nuances: contradiction can be in truth only, or in truth and falsehood, depending on whether existence of subject is considered and whether negation is absolute or modificatory. This shows the importance of precise usage.
This step deepens your understanding of logical contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المفهومان المتمانعان لذاتيهما، ولا تمانع بين التصورات فإن مفهومي الإنسان واللا إنسان مثلاً لا يتمانعان إلا إذا اعتبر ثبوتهما لشيء، وحينئذ يحصل هناك قضيتان متنافيتان صدقاً، وكذباً، وكذا قولنا حيوان ناطق وحيوان ليس بناطق على التقييد لا يتمانعان، إلا بملاحظة وقوع تلك النسبة إيجاباً، وارتفاعها سلباً أعني التصديقين. قوله (المتمانعان لذاتيهما) أي يكون ثبوت أحدهما مستلزماً لذات انتفاء الآخر وبالعكس. قوله : (فإن مفهومي الإنسان اللائق أن يقال : فإن تصوري الإنسان واللا إنسان لا يتمانعان كما في حواشي الأبهري إلا أن الشارح قصد المبالغة ببيان أن هذين المفهومين لا يتمانعان لا في الخارج، ولا في الذهن لتحققهما فيهما. النسخ قوله : ( يحصل هناك قضيتان متنافيتان) أي في الخارج وفي الذهن. قوله وقع في أكثر صدقاً وكذباً، وفي حواشي شرح مختصر الأصول صدقاً، وفي حواشي المطالع صدقاً لا كذبا، ولا تنافي بينها، لأنه إن لم يعتبر وجود الموضوع كانا متنافيين صدقا فقط، وإن اعتبر كانا متنافيين صدقاً وكذباً، وإن اعتبر اللا إنسان بمعنى السلب حتى يحصل من اعتبار ثبوته قضية سالبة المحمول كانا متنافيين صدقاً وكذباً، وإن اعتبر بمعنى العدول كانا متنافيين صدقاً فقط. قوله : ( إلا بملاحظة إلخ) التمانع بين المركبين التقييديين يتحقق على أنحاء ثلاثة باعتبار ثبوتهما لشيء وباعتبار وقوع تلك النسبة أولا وقوعها في الخارج وعلى التقديرين يتحقق قضيتان متنافيتان صدقاً فقط، أو صدقاً وكذباً على نحو ما مر في المفرد باعتبار ملاحظة وقوع تلك النسبة إيجاباً وارتفاعها سلباً وحينئذ يحصل تصديقان متناقضان، والشارح تعرض لهذا الاعتبار فقط لكونه أقرب لأن النسب التقييدية يعتبر فيها العلم ولذا قيل : الأوصاف قبل العلم.
The two mutually exclusive concepts in themselves, and there is no mutual exclusion between conceptions, for the concepts of human and non-human, for example, do not mutually exclude each other unless their subsistence for something is considered, and then there arise two propositions mutually contradictory in truth and falsehood. Similarly, our saying 'rational animal' and 'animal that is not rational' in a restricted sense do not mutually exclude each other except by observing the occurrence of that relation affirmatively and its removal negatively, i.e., the two assents. His saying: (the two mutually exclusive in themselves) means that the subsistence of one necessarily entails the non-existence of the other and vice versa. His saying: (for the concepts of human) it is appropriate to say: for the two concepts of human and non-human do not mutually exclude each other as in the glosses of al-Abharī, except that the commentator intended exaggeration by showing that these two concepts do not mutually exclude each other neither externally nor mentally due to their realization in both. His saying: (there arise two mutually contradictory propositions) i.e., externally and mentally. His saying: it occurs in most [copies] as 'in truth and falsehood', and in the glosses of the commentary on Mukhtasar al-Uṣūl as 'in truth', and in the glosses of al-Maṭāli‘ as 'in truth not falsehood', and there is no contradiction between them, because if the existence of the subject is not considered, they are contradictory in truth only; if it is considered, they are contradictory in truth and falsehood; if non-human is taken in the sense of negation so that from considering its subsistence a negative-predicate proposition arises, they are contradictory in truth and falsehood; if taken in the sense of modification, they are contradictory in truth only. His saying: (except by observing etc.) the mutual exclusion between restricted complexes occurs in three ways: by considering their subsistence for something and by considering the occurrence of that relation or its non-occurrence externally, and in both cases two propositions arise contradictory either in truth only or in truth and falsehood, similar to what passed with the simple term by considering the observation of the occurrence of that relation affirmatively and its removal negatively, and then two contradictory assents arise. The commentator only addressed this consideration because it is closer, since knowledge is considered in restricted relations, hence it is said: attributes before knowledge.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the concept of mutual exclusion (al-tamānu‘) and how it applies to conceptions (taṣawwurāt) versus propositions (qaḍāyā). The author explains that two concepts that are mutually exclusive in themselves (like human and non-human) do not contradict each other as mere concepts; contradiction only arises when they are considered as predicates for a subject, resulting in two propositions that are contradictory in truth and falsehood. The commentary then elaborates on different interpretations of the phrase 'truth and falsehood' from various glosses, explaining that the nature of the contradiction depends on whether the existence of the subject is considered and whether the negative term is interpreted as absolute negation (salb) or modification (ʿudūl). Finally, it applies the same logic to restricted complexes (murakkabāt taqyīdiyya) like 'rational animal' and its negation, showing that three types of contradiction can arise, but the commentator only focuses on the one involving the occurrence of the relation positively or negatively, because restricted relations involve knowledge.

تصديق
قضية
سلب
عدول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two concepts are mutually exclusive (like human and non-human), they cannot both be entertained in the mind at the same time.
Correction: The passage clarifies that concepts themselves do not contradict; they can coexist mentally. Contradiction only arises when they are asserted in a proposition about the same subject.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do the concepts 'human' and 'non-human' not contradict each other as mere concepts?
A) Because they are both affirmative concepts.
B) Because contradiction only arises when they are considered as predicates in a proposition with a subject.
C) Because they are both mental entities.
D) Because non-human is a negation and cannot be a concept.

Key Takeaway
Mutually exclusive concepts do not contradict as mere conceptions; contradiction requires a propositional context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that two of Allah's attributes (like His mercy and His justice) are contradictory simply because they seem like different concepts? This lesson shows that contradiction only arises when they are asserted as propositions about the same subject in the same respect. How does this principle change the way you think about divine attributes? Will you now pause before assuming a conflict?
?
When you read the Quran and encounter verses that seem to present opposite ideas (e.g., verses of hope and verses of fear), do you sometimes feel a conceptual tension? Reflect on a specific pair of verses. Can you hold both as true propositions about the same God without contradiction? What does this lesson teach you about the need to consider context and subject?
?
Think of a theological debate you have had or observed where someone insisted that their conceptual understanding was the only possible one. How might the distinction between tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (proposition) have prevented that argument? Will you apply this distinction in your own discussions to avoid false contradictions?

Why Conception Has No Contradictory
Proof that conception has no contradictory, with an objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core argument that conception (taṣawwur) has no contradictory because it is not a proposition, and how the author defends this against an objection regarding the nature of discrimination.

Core Idea
Conception has no contradictory because it is not a truth-bearing proposition.

This lesson presents the foundational proof that conception cannot have a contradictory. The author argues that discrimination (tamyīz), which is essential to conception, is a relation, not a proposition with a truth-value. An objection that discrimination is a quality is answered by explaining that 'discrimination' is used metaphorically to refer to the means of discrimination, not the relation itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the main claim and the flow of argument.
Pay attention to the distinction between proposition and relation.
2
Understand the core claim
The author asserts that conception has no contradictory because it is not a proposition.
3
Examine the objection
An objection is raised that discrimination must be a quality like affirmation/negation, not a relation.
4
Study the response
The author replies that 'discrimination' is a metaphor for the thing that discriminates, so it can be a relation.
This resolves the apparent inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يتم أن التصور لا نقيض له فحينئذ نقول تفسيره للتعريف منظور فيه لأن التمييز الذي هو إضافة بين المميز والمميز ليست قضية حتى يكون له نقيض فإن قلت : الإيجاب والسلب من قبيل الكيفيات، والتمييز من قبل الإضافة فكيف يكون إياهما قلت التميز مجاز عما به التميز وما ذكرنا قرينة المجاز بقي ها هنا بحثان الأول أنه لا تناقض بين الإدراكات ألا يرى أن الإيجاب والسلب مرتفعان عند الجهل البسيط والشك، والمتناقضان لا يصح ارتفاعهما، فكيف يقال : إن النفي والإثبات متناقضان ؟ الثاني : أنه إن أريد بما به التمييز الذي جعل مجازاً عنه نفس الصفة لم يصح قوله صفة توجب تمييزاً، إذ الشيء لا يوجب نفسه، وإن اكتفى بالمغايرة الاعتبارية كان مخالفا لما نقل عنه في الحواشي من نقيض التمييز لا نقيض الصفة أو المتعلق وإن أريد أمر آخر يلزم تحقق أمور ثلاثة الصفة والتمييز، وشيء ثالث بينهما به التميز ولا يخفى بطلانه، اللهم إلا أن يجاب عن أصل الاعتراض بمنع كون الإيجاب والسلب من قبيل الكيفيات فتأمل . قوله: (متنافيتان صدقاً وكذباً إن أخذ الإنسان بمعنى السلب حتى تكون القضية أن المراد اللذين أشير بهذين القولين إليهما بعد رعاية شروط التناقض فيهما، وإطلاق النقيض على أطراف القضايا سواء كانت تلك الأطراف بمعنى السلب، أو بمعنى العدول مجاز على التأويل، لا يقال : فعلى هذا جميع التصورات علم مع أن بعضها غير مطابق، لأنا نقول : لا يوصف التصور بعدم المطابقة أصلاً . قوله مجاز على التأويل) أي التأويل في مفهوم النقيضين بأن يراد بهما المتباعدان غاية التباعد سواء كانا متمانعين أولاً، أو التأويل بأن الحكم على الأطراف بالنقيض باعتبار الحكم المقارن لتصوراتها، وهو أن هذه الصورة لذلك الشي، والأولى أوجه وإلى الثاني ذهب الفاضل الأبهري .
And by this it is established that conception has no contradictory. Then we say: his interpretation of the definition is subject to consideration, because the discrimination which is a relation between the discriminator and the discriminated is not a proposition such that it has a contradictory. If you say: Affirmation and negation are of the category of qualities, and discrimination is of the category of relation, so how can it be them? I say: Discrimination is a metaphor for that by which discrimination is made, and what we mentioned is the indicator of metaphor. Two investigations remain here: First, there is no contradiction between perceptions; do you not see that affirmation and negation are both absent in simple ignorance and doubt, and two contradictories cannot both be absent, so how can it be said that negation and affirmation are contradictories? Second: If by 'that by which discrimination is made' which is taken metaphorically, what is meant is the attribute itself, then his saying 'an attribute that necessitates discrimination' is not correct, since a thing does not necessitate itself. And if one is satisfied with conceptual difference, it contradicts what is transmitted from him in the glosses that 'the contradictory of discrimination is not the contradictory of the attribute or the object'. And if something else is intended, then three things must exist: the attribute, discrimination, and a third thing between them by which discrimination is made, and its falsehood is not hidden. Unless the original objection is answered by preventing that affirmation and negation are of the category of qualities, so reflect. His saying: (mutually contradictory in truth and falsehood) if 'human' is taken in the sense of negation so that the proposition is... what is intended are the two [meanings] indicated by these two statements after observing the conditions of contradiction in them, and applying the term 'contradictory' to the parts of propositions, whether those parts are in the sense of negation or modification, is metaphorical according to interpretation. It is not said: Accordingly, all conceptions are knowledge despite some of them being non-conformant, for we say: Conception is not described by non-conformance at all. His saying: (metaphorical according to interpretation) i.e., interpretation in the concept of the two contradictories: either that they are intended as extremely distant, whether they are mutually exclusive or not, or the interpretation that the judgment about the parts as contradictory is by virtue of the judgment accompanying their conceptions, namely that this image is for that thing. The first is more appropriate, and the second is held by al-Fāḍil al-Abharī.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage provides a proof that conception (taṣawwur) has no contradictory, because conception is not a proposition; discrimination (tamyīz) is a relation, not a proposition with a contradictory. An objection is raised: if discrimination is equivalent to affirmation and negation (which are qualities), then it cannot be a relation. The response is that 'discrimination' is a metaphor for 'that by which discrimination is made' (i.e., the act or attribute). Two remaining investigations follow. The first objection: if affirmation and negation are contradictories, they cannot both be absent, yet in simple ignorance and doubt both are absent, so they cannot be true contradictories. The second objection: if 'that by which discrimination' is the attribute itself, then the attribute would necessitate itself, which is absurd; if it is a conceptual difference, it conflicts with glosses; if it is a third thing, it multiplies entities unnecessarily. The answer suggests that the original objection can be rejected by denying that affirmation and negation belong to the category of qualities. The passage then discusses the metaphorical application of 'contradictory' to propositional parts, explaining two interpretations: one by extreme distance, another by accompanying judgment. The first is preferred, the second attributed to al-Abharī.

تصور
نقيض
تمييز
إضافة
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a concept can have a contradictory, like the concept of 'human' is contradicted by 'non-human'.
Correction: The passage explains that contradiction only applies to propositions, not to concepts themselves. A concept is not a truth-bearing statement.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say conception has no contradictory?
A) Because conception is always true.
B) Because conception is not a proposition but a relation.
C) Because conception is a simple idea.
D) Because conception can be both true and false.

Key Takeaway
Conception has no contradictory because it is not a proposition; discrimination is a relation, not a truth-bearing statement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel that your mental image of God contradicts someone else's? How does knowing that conceptions themselves have no contradictory free you from such confusion? Will you now examine whether the disagreement is at the level of conception or assent?
?
When you hold a conception (e.g., of tawhid), are you tempted to assert it as a proposition in debate? How can you distinguish between the conception and its propositional expression in your heart? Commit to noticing this distinction in your next discussion.
?
What is one conception you hold about the Quran that you have never clarified whether it is a simple concept or a proposition? How does this lesson compel you to re-examine it? Take a moment to identify one such concept and ask yourself: is this a mere image or a truth-claim?

First Investigation: No Contradiction Between Perceptions
First investigation: no contradiction between perceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against considering affirmation and negation as contradictories in perception, based on their possible simultaneous absence.

Core Idea
Affirmation and negation cannot be contradictories because both can be absent in doubt.

In this part, the author raises an important objection: if affirmation and negation were genuine contradictories, they could never both be absent. Yet, in states of simple ignorance or doubt, a person neither affirms nor negates a proposition. This shows that the relationship between affirmation and negation in perception is not one of logical contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the investigation
Read the Arabic passage that presents the first investigation.
2
Understand the argument
The argument uses the fact that in simple ignorance and doubt, both affirmation and negation are absent, which contradicts the definition of contradictories that cannot both be absent.
This is a classical logical point.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this objection matters for the discussion about conception and its contradictory.
It challenges the assumption that negation and affirmation are simple qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And by this it is established that conception has no contradictory. Then we say: his interpretation of the definition is subject to consideration, because the discrimination which is a relation between the discriminator and the discriminated is not a proposition such that it has a contradictory. If you say: Affirmation and negation are of the category of qualities, and discrimination is of the category of relation, so how can it be them? I say: Discrimination is a metaphor for that by which discrimination is made, and what we mentioned is the indicator of metaphor. Two investigations remain here: First, there is no contradiction between perceptions; do you not see that affirmation and negation are both absent in simple ignorance and doubt, and two contradictories cannot both be absent, so how can it be said that negation and affirmation are contradictories? Second Investigation: The Attribute and Discrimination
Second investigation analyzing the interpretation of 'that by which discrimination'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection concerning the interpretation of 'that by which discrimination is made', and the three possible readings.

Core Idea
If discrimination is an attribute, it cannot necessitate itself; other interpretations lead to problems.

The second investigation delves into the meaning of 'that by which discrimination is made'. The author considers three possibilities: that it refers to the attribute itself, that it is conceptually distinct, or that it is a third entity. Each possibility is examined and found problematic, leading to the suggestion that the original objection may be avoided by denying that affirmation and negation are qualities.

Guided Path
1
Read the second investigation
Read the Arabic text that presents the three possibilities and their critiques.
2
Analyze the first possibility
If 'that by which' means the attribute itself, then the phrase 'an attribute that necessitates discrimination' becomes problematic because a thing cannot necessitate itself.
This is a self-reference issue.
3
Analyze the second possibility
If it is a conceptual distinction, it conflicts with glosses that say the contradictory of discrimination is not the contradictory of the attribute.
4
Analyze the third possibility
If a third entity is meant, then three things are required, which is untenable.
The author then suggests a way out by rejecting the premise that affirmation and negation are qualities.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يتم أن التصور لا نقيض له فحينئذ نقول تفسيره للتعريف منظور فيه لأن التمييز الذي هو إضافة بين المميز والمميز ليست قضية حتى يكون له نقيض فإن قلت : الإيجاب والسلب من قبيل الكيفيات، والتمييز من قبل الإضافة فكيف يكون إياهما قلت التميز مجاز عما به التميز وما ذكرنا قرينة المجاز بقي ها هنا بحثان الأول أنه لا تناقض بين الإدراكات ألا يرى أن الإيجاب والسلب مرتفعان عند الجهل البسيط والشك، والمتناقضان لا يصح ارتفاعهما، فكيف يقال : إن النفي والإثبات متناقضان ؟ الثاني : أنه إن أريد بما به التمييز الذي جعل مجازاً عنه نفس الصفة لم يصح قوله صفة توجب تمييزاً، إذ الشيء لا يوجب نفسه، وإن اكتفى بالمغايرة الاعتبارية كان مخالفا لما نقل عنه في الحواشي من نقيض التمييز لا نقيض الصفة أو المتعلق وإن أريد أمر آخر يلزم تحقق أمور ثلاثة الصفة والتمييز، وشيء ثالث بينهما به التميز ولا يخفى بطلانه، اللهم إلا أن يجاب عن أصل الاعتراض بمنع كون الإيجاب والسلب من قبيل الكيفيات فتأمل . قوله: (متنافيتان صدقاً وكذباً إن أخذ الإنسان بمعنى السلب حتى تكون القضية أن المراد اللذين أشير بهذين القولين إليهما بعد رعاية شروط التناقض فيهما، وإطلاق النقيض على أطراف القضايا سواء كانت تلك الأطراف بمعنى السلب، أو بمعنى العدول مجاز على التأويل، لا يقال : فعلى هذا جميع التصورات علم مع أن بعضها غير مطابق، لأنا نقول : لا يوصف التصور بعدم المطابقة أصلاً . قوله مجاز على التأويل) أي التأويل في مفهوم النقيضين بأن يراد بهما المتباعدان غاية التباعد سواء كانا متمانعين أولاً، أو التأويل بأن الحكم على الأطراف بالنقيض باعتبار الحكم المقارن لتصوراتها، وهو أن هذه الصورة لذلك الشي، والأولى أوجه وإلى الثاني ذهب الفاضل الأبهري .
And by this it is established that conception has no contradictory. Then we say: his interpretation of the definition is subject to consideration, because the discrimination which is a relation between the discriminator and the discriminated is not a proposition such that it has a contradictory. If you say: Affirmation and negation are of the category of qualities, and discrimination is of the category of relation, so how can it be them? I say: Discrimination is a metaphor for that by which discrimination is made, and what we mentioned is the indicator of metaphor. Two investigations remain here: First, there is no contradiction between perceptions; do you not see that affirmation and negation are both absent in simple ignorance and doubt, and two contradictories cannot both be absent, so how can it be said that negation and affirmation are contradictories? Second: If by 'that by which discrimination is made' which is taken metaphorically, what is meant is the attribute itself, then his saying 'an attribute that necessitates discrimination' is not correct, since a thing does not necessitate itself. And if one is satisfied with conceptual difference, it contradicts what is transmitted from him in the glosses that 'the contradictory of discrimination is not the contradictory of the attribute or the object'. And if something else is intended, then three things must exist: the attribute, discrimination, and a third thing between them by which discrimination is made, and its falsehood is not hidden. Unless the original objection is answered by preventing that affirmation and negation are of the category of qualities, so reflect. His saying: (mutually contradictory in truth and falsehood) if 'human' is taken in the sense of negation so that the proposition is... what is intended are the two [meanings] indicated by these two statements after observing the conditions of contradiction in them, and applying the term 'contradictory' to the parts of propositions, whether those parts are in the sense of negation or modification, is metaphorical according to interpretation. It is not said: Accordingly, all conceptions are knowledge despite some of them being non-conformant, for we say: Conception is not described by non-conformance at all. His saying: (metaphorical according to interpretation) i.e., interpretation in the concept of the two contradictories: either that they are intended as extremely distant, whether they are mutually exclusive or not, or the interpretation that the judgment about the parts as contradictory is by virtue of the judgment accompanying their conceptions, namely that this image is for that thing. The first is more appropriate, and the second is held by al-Fāḍil al-Abharī.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage provides a proof that conception (taṣawwur) has no contradictory, because conception is not a proposition; discrimination (tamyīz) is a relation, not a proposition with a contradictory. An objection is raised: if discrimination is equivalent to affirmation and negation (which are qualities), then it cannot be a relation. The response is that 'discrimination' is a metaphor for 'that by which discrimination is made' (i.e., the act or attribute). Two remaining investigations follow. The first objection: if affirmation and negation are contradictories, they cannot both be absent, yet in simple ignorance and doubt both are absent, so they cannot be true contradictories. The second objection: if 'that by which discrimination' is the attribute itself, then the attribute would necessitate itself, which is absurd; if it is a conceptual difference, it conflicts with glosses; if it is a third thing, it multiplies entities unnecessarily. The answer suggests that the original objection can be rejected by denying that affirmation and negation belong to the category of qualities. The passage then discusses the metaphorical application of 'contradictory' to propositional parts, explaining two interpretations: one by extreme distance, another by accompanying judgment. The first is preferred, the second attributed to al-Abharī.

Key Terms
صفة
تمييز
مغايرة اعتبارية
حواشي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second investigation, what is a potential problem if 'that by which discrimination' is taken to be the attribute itself?
A) It would lead to an infinite regress.
B) It would make discrimination unnecessary.
C) It would require the attribute to necessitate itself.
D) It would contradict the definition of conception.

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of 'that by which discrimination' leads to logical difficulties, suggesting the need to reject the premise that affirmation and negation are qualities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that your good qualities (like knowledge or piety) automatically produce the desired result in your life? How does this lesson's warning against self-necessitation expose that presumption? Will you commit to checking your intentions and relying solely on Allah's grace?
?
When you encounter a complex argument in Islamic theology, do you tend to simplify it and miss the logical pitfalls? This lesson shows how details matter. In what area of your faith have you glossed over distinctions that actually lead to error? Will you take time to study deeply?
?
The author suggests that rejecting that 'affirmation and negation are qualities' can solve the problem. Is there a belief you hold that you've never questioned but which might undermine your understanding of Allah? Are you willing to examine foundational assumptions about His attributes?

Metaphorical Use of 'Contradictory' for Propositional Parts
Discussion on the metaphorical application of 'contradictory' to parts of propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'contradictory' can be applied metaphorically to parts of propositions, with two possible interpretations.

Core Idea
Contradictory is a metaphor for extreme distance or by virtue of accompanying judgments.

This section addresses the metaphorical application of the term 'contradictory' to the parts of propositions, such as 'human' and 'not-human'. The author explains that this usage is not literal but metaphorical, and proposes two interpretations: one based on extreme distance, and another based on the accompanying judgment. The first interpretation is deemed more appropriate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on metaphorical usage
Read the Arabic text that discusses the application of 'contradictory' to propositional parts.
2
Understand the two interpretations
The first interpretation: contradictory means extremely distant, whether mutually exclusive or not. The second: it refers to the accompanying judgment that the image corresponds to an object.
3
Note the preferred view
The author prefers the first interpretation and attributes the second to al-Fāḍil al-Abharī.

Two Views on the Distinction between Knowing by a Face and Knowing the Face
Presents the disagreement on the distinction between two types of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing views of the earlier and later scholars regarding the distinction between 'ilm al-shay' bi al-wajh and 'ilm wajh al-shay.

Core Idea
The distinction is either by essence or by consideration.

This passage introduces a key disagreement in Islamic logic and theology: whether knowing something by a particular aspect and knowing that aspect itself are distinct in essence or merely in our way of considering them. The author presents both positions, which have practical implications for reasoning about the unknown.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of knowledge
Read carefully the terms 'ilm al-shay' bi al-wajh and 'ilm wajh al-shay. They both involve 'face' but differ in focus.
The first is knowing the thing by its aspect, the second is knowing the aspect itself.
2
Understand the muta'akhkhirun view
The later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) say the distinction is by essence. In the first, the face itself is in the mind as an instrument; in the second, the image of the face is known without reference to the thing.
This is a real, objective distinction.
3
Understand the mutaqaddimun view
The earlier scholars (mutaqaddimun) say the distinction is by consideration. The difference depends on whether one considers the aspect as predicated of the thing (as in quantified propositions) or not (as in natural propositions).
This is a conceptual distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ومنهم من أثبت إلخ ) اعلم أنهم اختلفوا في علم الشيء بالوجه، وعلم وجه الشيء لا تحقيق له أنه لا تغاير بينهما أصلا وقال المتأخرون : بالتغاير بالذات إذ الأول الحاصل في الذهن نفس الوجه، وهو آلة ملاحظة الشيء والشيء معلوم بالذات، وفي الثاني الحاصل في الذهن صورة الوجه، وهو المعلوم بالذات من غير التفات إلى الشيء ذي الوجه، وقال المتقدمون : التغاير بينهما بالاعتبار إذ لا شك في أنه لا يمكن أن يشاهد بالضاحك أمر سواه، إلا أنه إذا عتبر صدقه عليه أمر واتحاده معه كما في موضوع القضية المحصورة كان علم الشيء بالوجه، وإذا اعتبر مع قطع النظر عن ذلك كان علم الوجه كما في موضوع القضية الطبيعية، إذا علمت هذا، فاعلم أن عود الإمام إما مبني على عدم التغاير مطلقا، وتقريره أن الشيء المشعور به من وجه دون وجه لا يطلب بوجهيه لأن الوجه المعلوم ،معلوم والوجه المجهول مجهول فلا يطلب شيء منهما ، فلا يمكن طلبه وإما مبني على رأي المتقدمين وتقريره أن المطلوب إذا كان مشعوراً به بوجه دون وجه كان المعلوم والمجهول في الحقيقة هما الوجهان، لكن من حيث صدقهما على ذلك الشيء، واتحادهما به والمعلوم معلوم مطلقاً، والمجهول مجهول مطلقاً لا يمكن طلب شيء منهما
His saying: (And among them is one who affirmed etc.) Know that they disagreed about knowing the thing by the face, and knowing the face of the thing: there is no verification that there is no distinction between them at all, and the later scholars said: distinction by essence (bi al-dhat), because the first is the face itself in the mind, and it is the instrument of observing the thing, and the thing is known by essence; and the second is the image of the face in the mind, and it is the known by essence without attention to the thing possessing the face. And the earlier scholars said: the distinction between them is by consideration (bi al-i'tibar), because there is no doubt that it is not possible to perceive by 'the laughing one' something other than it, except that when one considers its truth about it and its union with it, as in the subject of a quantified proposition (al-qadiyya al-mahsura), it is knowing the thing by the face; and when one considers disregarding that, it is knowing the face, as in the subject of a natural proposition (al-qadiyya al-tabi'iyya). If you have known this, then know that the return of the Imam is either based on absolute non-distinction, and its explanation is that the thing perceived by a face without another face is not sought by its two faces, because the known face is known and the unknown face is unknown, so nothing of them is sought, so it cannot be sought; or based on the view of the earlier scholars, and its explanation is that the sought-object, when perceived by a face without another face, the known and unknown are in reality the two faces, but in terms of their truth about that thing, and their union with it, and the known is absolutely known, and the unknown is absolutely unknown, so it is not possible to seek anything of them.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of two opposing views regarding the types of knowledge: 'knowing a thing by its face' (ilm al-shay' bi al-wajh) and 'knowing the face itself' (ilm wajh al-shay). The author first states that there is no verification of no distinction between them, then presents the positions of the later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) and earlier scholars (mutaqaddimun). The muta'akhkhirun hold that the distinction is by essence (taghayyur bi al-dhat): the first is the face itself in the mind, serving as an instrument to observe the thing, while the second is the image of the face, known without attending to the thing. The mutaqaddimun hold that the distinction is by consideration (taghayyur bi al-i'tibar): the difference lies in whether one considers the face as predicated of the thing and united with it (as in a quantified proposition) or disregards that (as in a natural proposition). The passage then applies these views to the shubha (sophistical argument) about seeking something known by one face but unknown by another. If the Imam's position is based on absolute non-distinction, then the thing perceived by one face cannot be sought by either face because one is known and the other unknown. If based on the mutaqaddimun's view, then the known and unknown are actually the two faces, and since the known is absolutely known and the unknown absolutely unknown, nothing can be sought from them. This sets up the problem to which answers will be given.

Key Terms
علم الشيء بالوجه
علم وجه الشيء
المتأخرون
التغاير بالذات
المتقدمون
التغاير بالاعتبار
القضية المحصورة
القضية الطبيعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the muta'akhkhirun, what distinguishes 'ilm al-shay' bi al-wajh from 'ilm wajh al-shay?
A) They are identical with no distinction.
B) Distinction by consideration (bi al-i'tibar).
C) Distinction by essence (bi al-dhat).
D) The first is known by essence, the second by accident.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between the two types of knowledge depends on whether the aspect is considered as an instrument to know the thing or as the known itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek to know Allah, do you often stop at knowing His attributes (the 'face') without letting them lead you to knowing Him directly? What would change if you used each attribute as an instrument to know the Essence?
?
Consider a time you studied a religious concept. Were you content with knowing the concept itself, or did you strive to see it as a window to something greater? Which approach does the earlier scholars' view encourage?
?
The later scholars say the face is an instrument; the earlier say it is united with the thing. In your daily life, do you treat the means of worship (e.g., prayer, dhikr) as ends in themselves or as instruments to draw nearer to Allah? How can you shift your intention?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
5 min
Application to the Problem of Seeking the Unknown by a Known Face
Applies the two views to the shubha about seeking the known-by-one-face-unknown-by-another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how both the view of absolute non-distinction and the view of distinction by consideration lead to the same conclusion that seeking something perceived by one face but unknown by another is impossible.

Core Idea
Both views lead to the same impasse: the known and unknown faces cannot be sought.

After presenting the two views on the types of knowledge, the author applies them to a logical shubha (sophistical argument) about seeking something known by one aspect but unknown by another. This lesson shows how both the view of absolute non-distinction and the view of distinction by consideration result in the impossibility of such a search.

Guided Path
1
Understand the shubha
The problem is about seeking something that is perceived by one face (known) but unknown by another face. The two faces represent different aspects.
The shubha is that such a thing cannot be sought because the known face is already known and the unknown face is unknown.
2
See how each view leads to impossibility
If based on absolute non-distinction, the thing itself is not sought because both faces are the same; if based on the mutaqaddimun view, the known and unknown are the two faces, and since both are absolute, nothing can be sought.
In both interpretations, the search is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ومنهم من أثبت إلخ ) اعلم أنهم اختلفوا في علم الشيء بالوجه، وعلم وجه الشيء لا تحقيق له أنه لا تغاير بينهما أصلا وقال المتأخرون : بالتغاير بالذات إذ الأول الحاصل في الذهن نفس الوجه، وهو آلة ملاحظة الشيء والشيء معلوم بالذات، وفي الثاني الحاصل في الذهن صورة الوجه، وهو المعلوم بالذات من غير التفات إلى الشيء ذي الوجه، وقال المتقدمون : التغاير بينهما بالاعتبار إذ لا شك في أنه لا يمكن أن يشاهد بالضاحك أمر سواه، إلا أنه إذا عتبر صدقه عليه أمر واتحاده معه كما في موضوع القضية المحصورة كان علم الشيء بالوجه، وإذا اعتبر مع قطع النظر عن ذلك كان علم الوجه كما في موضوع القضية الطبيعية، إذا علمت هذا، فاعلم أن عود الإمام إما مبني على عدم التغاير مطلقا، وتقريره أن الشيء المشعور به من وجه دون وجه لا يطلب بوجهيه لأن الوجه المعلوم ،معلوم والوجه المجهول مجهول فلا يطلب شيء منهما ، فلا يمكن طلبه وإما مبني على رأي المتقدمين وتقريره أن المطلوب إذا كان مشعوراً به بوجه دون وجه كان المعلوم والمجهول في الحقيقة هما الوجهان، لكن من حيث صدقهما على ذلك الشيء، واتحادهما به والمعلوم معلوم مطلقاً، والمجهول مجهول مطلقاً لا يمكن طلب شيء منهما
His saying: (And among them is one who affirmed etc.) Know that they disagreed about knowing the thing by the face, and knowing the face of the thing: there is no verification that there is no distinction between them at all, and the later scholars said: distinction by essence (bi al-dhat), because the first is the face itself in the mind, and it is the instrument of observing the thing, and the thing is known by essence; and the second is the image of the face in the mind, and it is the known by essence without attention to the thing possessing the face. And the earlier scholars said: the distinction between them is by consideration (bi al-i'tibar), because there is no doubt that it is not possible to perceive by 'the laughing one' something other than it, except that when one considers its truth about it and its union with it, as in the subject of a quantified proposition (al-qadiyya al-mahsura), it is knowing the thing by the face; and when one considers disregarding that, it is knowing the face, as in the subject of a natural proposition (al-qadiyya al-tabi'iyya). If you have known this, then know that the return of the Imam is either based on absolute non-distinction, and its explanation is that the thing perceived by a face without another face is not sought by its two faces, because the known face is known and the unknown face is unknown, so nothing of them is sought, so it cannot be sought; or based on the view of the earlier scholars, and its explanation is that the sought-object, when perceived by a face without another face, the known and unknown are in reality the two faces, but in terms of their truth about that thing, and their union with it, and the known is absolutely known, and the unknown is absolutely unknown, so it is not possible to seek anything of them.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of two opposing views regarding the types of knowledge: 'knowing a thing by its face' (ilm al-shay' bi al-wajh) and 'knowing the face itself' (ilm wajh al-shay). The author first states that there is no verification of no distinction between them, then presents the positions of the later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) and earlier scholars (mutaqaddimun). The muta'akhkhirun hold that the distinction is by essence (taghayyur bi al-dhat): the first is the face itself in the mind, serving as an instrument to observe the thing, while the second is the image of the face, known without attending to the thing. The mutaqaddimun hold that the distinction is by consideration (taghayyur bi al-i'tibar): the difference lies in whether one considers the face as predicated of the thing and united with it (as in a quantified proposition) or disregards that (as in a natural proposition). The passage then applies these views to the shubha (sophistical argument) about seeking something known by one face but unknown by another. If the Imam's position is based on absolute non-distinction, then the thing perceived by one face cannot be sought by either face because one is known and the other unknown. If based on the mutaqaddimun's view, then the known and unknown are actually the two faces, and since the known is absolutely known and the unknown absolutely unknown, nothing can be sought from them. This sets up the problem to which answers will be given.

المتقدمون
التغاير بالاعتبار
القضية المحصورة
القضية الطبيعية

Checkpoint
According to the application based on the mutaqaddimun view, what do the known and unknown actually refer to?
A) The thing itself.
B) The two faces.
C) The instrument of observation.
D) The absolute.

Key Takeaway
The shubha's resolution depends on the underlying view of the distinction, but both lead to the same conclusion of impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often claim to know something about your faith, but are you aware of the faces you do not know? In what area of your belief are you relying on a single 'face' while ignoring deeper unknowns? What will you do today to seek the unknown face, even if it humbles you?
?
The passage shows that partial knowledge cannot lead to full seeking. Where in your life are you satisfied with a partial understanding of Allah's attributes, allowing yourself to stop seeking? How will you push yourself to confront the 'unknown face' that you have avoided?
?
You may think you are seeking truth, but this argument reveals that if your knowledge is incomplete, your seeking is futile. Is there a truth about yourself or your Creator that you have been avoiding because you only know one side? What step will you take today to face the unknown face with sincerity?

The Earlier Scholars' Answer to the Shubha
Answer to the shubha from the earlier scholars' perspective

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the earlier scholars (mutaqaddimun) refute the shubha by arguing that the unknown face is not absolutely unknown because it is known through its union with the known face.

Core Idea
The unknown face is known through its union with the known face.

The shubha claims that seeking something with both a known and unknown face is impossible because one cannot seek what is unknown. The earlier scholars respond by rejecting the premise that the unknown face is completely unknown. In this lesson, you will see their precise argument: the unknown face is known in terms of its union with the known face, so no third thing is needed.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer based on the earlier scholars
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'فإن أجيب على رأي المتقدمين' and its translation. Notice the structure: the answer is presented as a concession that the unknown face is not absolutely unknown.
The key phrase is 'لا نسلم أن الوجه المجهول مجهول مطلقاً'.
2
Understand the reasoning about union
The passage explains that the unknown face is known from the aspect of its union with the known face. This refutes the need for a third thing. Ask yourself: How does union resolve the problem?
The union makes the unknown face accessible via the known face.
3
Connect to the broader debate
This answer is specifically for the earlier school. It shows that no third entity is required, countering the objection from Naqd al-Muhassal.
The earlier scholars do not posit a third thing; they resolve the problem within the two faces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أجيب علي رأي المتقدمين فالجواب ما ذكره المصنف، وهو أنا لا نسلم أن الوجه المجهول مجهول مطلقاً، لأنه إذا كان الوجه المعلوم معلوماً من حيث الاتحاد بذلك الشيء، والمجهول مجهولاً من تلك الحيثية كان الوجه المجهول معلوماً من حيث اتحاد الوجه المعلوم به ولا معنى حينئذ لجواب نقد المحصل إذ المطلوب ليس أمرا ثالثا عندهم، وإن أجيب على رأي المتأخرين فالجواب ما ذكره في النقد وهو أنه لا يلزم من امتناع طلب الوجهين امتناع طلب الأمر الثالث الذي هو ذو الوجهين، فكما أن الوجه المعلوم صار آلة الملاحظة الشيء، ومرآة لانكشافه كذلك، يطلب ذلك الشيء بأن يصير أمر آخر آلة لملاحظته، ومرآة له وتفصيله أن عارض الشيء قد يلاحظ في نفسه، فيكون العارض معلوماً، والشيء مغفولاً عنه بالكلية، وقد يجعل آلة لملاحظته، وحينئذ يكون معلوماً باعتبار ذلك العارض مجهولاً باعتبار آخر، فيتحد المعلوم والمجهول لكنه معلوم من حيثية ومجهول من حيثية أخرى، ولا استمالة فيه ولا معنى حينئذ لجواب المصنف، إذ ليس المطلوب عندهم الوجه حتى يجاب بأن الوجه المجهول ليس مجهولاً مطلقاً، فتدبر والله الموفق وأما ما ذكره الشارح من أنه الزام للإمام حيث اعترف بمغايرة الوجهين لذي الوجهين ففيه إن العبارة المشعرة بالتغاير ليس إلا قوله : لكن لما اجتمعا في شيء واحد، إذ لا بد من التغاير بين الظرف والمظروف، وهو لا يقتضي التغاير بالذات لجواز أن يكون مراده لما اجتمعا في شيء واحد هو الكل من حيث هو كل بل نقول : لا بد من حمل كلامه على ذلك، إذ لو حمل على التغاير بالذات لم يتم التقريب إذ لا يلزم من انتفاء الإجمال في الوجهين انتفاء الإجمال في الشيء ذي الوجهين وحينئذ لا يتم الإلزام إذ ليس في الحقيقة إلا الوجهان، ولا يمكن طلب شيء منهما .
If it is answered according to the view of the earlier scholars, then the answer is what the author (al-Misri?) mentioned, namely that we do not concede that the unknown face is absolutely unknown, because if the known face is known in terms of its union with that thing, and the unknown is unknown from that aspect, then the unknown face is known in terms of the union of the known face with it, and there is no meaning then for the answer of Naqd al-Muhassal, since the sought-object is not a third thing according to them. And if it is answered according to the view of the later scholars, then the answer is what is mentioned in al-Naqd, namely that the impossibility of seeking the two faces does not entail the impossibility of seeking the third thing which is the possessor of the two faces. For just as the known face became an instrument for observing the thing and a mirror for its disclosure, likewise that thing is sought by becoming another thing an instrument for its observation and a mirror for it. And its detail is that an accident of a thing may be observed in itself, so the accident becomes known and the thing is completely disregarded; and it may be made an instrument for observing it, and at that time it becomes known in respect of that accident and unknown in another respect, so the known and unknown unite, but it is known from one aspect and unknown from another aspect; and there is no improbability in it, and there is no meaning then for the answer of the author, because the sought-object according to them is not the face such that it is answered that the unknown face is not absolutely unknown. So reflect, and God is the grantor of success. And as for what the commentator (al-Sharif?) mentioned, that it is an objection to the Imam where he acknowledged the distinction of the two faces from the possessor of the two faces, there is in it that the expression indicating distinction is only his saying: 'but when they are united in one thing', for there must be distinction between the container and the contained, and this does not entail distinction in essence (bi al-dhat), because it is possible that his intention by 'when they are united in one thing' is the whole in terms of being a whole; rather we say: it is necessary to interpret his words in that way, because if it were interpreted as distinction in essence, the approximation would not be complete, since the negation of ambiguity in the two faces does not necessitate the negation of ambiguity in the thing possessing the two faces, and then the objection is not complete, for in reality there are only the two faces, and it is not possible to seek anything of them.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct answers to the shubha (sophistical argument) about seeking something known by one face and unknown by another, depending on which school's view of 'ilm al-shay' bi al-wajh and 'ilm wajh al-shay is adopted. First, according to the earlier scholars (mutaqaddimun), the answer is that the unknown face is not absolutely unknown because it is known through its union with the known face, so there is no need for a third thing. This refutes the objection based on Naqd al-Muhassal. Second, according to the later scholars (muta'akhkhirun), the answer is that the impossibility of seeking the two faces does not entail the impossibility of seeking the third thing (the thing itself). The known face serves as an instrument; similarly, the thing can be sought by making another accident an instrument. The author then discusses a point by the commentator (al-Sharif?) that this is an objection to the Imam, but he argues that the Imam's statement about the union of the two faces in one thing does not imply essential distinction; it is merely distinction between container and contained, or referring to the whole as a whole. Thus, the objection fails, and only the two faces exist.

الشبهة
الوجه المجهول
الوجه المعلوم
نقد المحصل
