Second Answer: Restricting the Subject to Tasawwur
Second answer restricts the subject to tasawwur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how restricting the subject of the premises to tasawwur (concept) resolves the objection and why the alternative conversion to a universal affirmative proposition is not beneficial.

Core Idea
Restricting the subject to tasawwur keeps the premises true even when the subject doesn't exist.

In this passage, the commentator presents the second answer to a logical objection about seeking knowledge. The objection claimed that premises requiring the subject to exist would lead to contradictions. The answer is to restrict the subject of both premises to the realm of concepts (tasawwur), thereby allowing the converse to hold without assuming actual existence. Read on to see how this subtle logical move saves the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation to grasp the meaning.
Pay attention to the logical terminology.
2
Identify the two modified premises
Notice how the original premises are changed by restricting the subject to tasawwur. The first becomes 'Every perceived entity absolutely has its seeking impossible' and its simple converse becomes 'Some of what is not perceived absolutely does not have its seeking impossible'.
3
Understand why this avoids contradiction
The text explains that the converse can be true even if no such subject exists, because the subject is qualified by a negated perception from some aspect.
This hinges on the nature of tasawwur.
4
Examine the rejected alternative
The author mentions a proposed conversion to a universal affirmative with both terms negated, but dismisses it because such a proposition acts like a simple negative and doesn't imply subject existence.
5
Conclude the correct answer
The second answer is affirmed as the correct one.
This resolves the objection successfully.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وأجيب بتقييد الموضوع فيهما بالتصور أخرى) أي يسلب الموضوع في كل منهما عن التصور. فيصير قولنا: كل مشعور به مطلقاً أي من جميع الوجوه يمتنع طلبه، وعكسه المستوي بعض ما ليس مشعوراً به مطلقاً لا يمتنع طلبه، وهذا لا ينافي الأصل لجواز صدقه باعتبار أن يكون ذلك البعض مشعوراً به من وجه دون وجه كالمطلوب التصديقي. وأما انعكاس هذه الحملية إلى موجبة كلية سالبة الطرفين كما أثبته شارح المطالع فلا يفيد هاهنا لأن الموجبة السالبة الطرفين في حكم السالبة البسيطة في عدم اقتضاء وجود الموضوع. فالحق أن الجواب هو الثاني.
His statement: 'And it was answered by restricting the subject in both of them to tasawwur in another way' — meaning that the subject in each of them is negated with respect to tasawwur. So our statement becomes: 'Every perceived entity absolutely (i.e., from all respects) has its seeking impossible,' and its simple converse: 'Some of what is not perceived absolutely has its seeking not impossible,' and this does not contradict the original [premises] due to the possibility of its truth considering that that part is perceived in some respect and not in another, like the desired judgment (al-maṭlūb al-taṣdīqī). As for the conversion of this categorical premise into a universal affirmative with both terms negated, as established by the author of al-Maṭāli‘, it does not benefit here because the universal affirmative with both terms negated is in the ruling of a simple negative in not implying the existence of the subject. So the truth is that the answer is the second.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents the second answer to the objection. The answer is to restrict the subject of the two premises to tasawwur (conceptual knowledge). By doing so, the premise 'Everything perceived has its seeking impossible' is understood as applying to all perceived things absolutely, and its converse becomes a particular negative that does not require an existent subject. The conversion to a universal affirmative with both terms negated, as some scholars proposed, is not useful here because such a proposition functions like a simple negative and does not imply the existence of the subject. Therefore, the second answer is considered the correct one.

التصور
الموجبة الكلية
السلبية البسيطة
المطلوب التصديقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negating the subject of a proposition removes its logical force entirely.
Correction: This answer shows that negating the subject only with respect to tasawwur allows the converse to be true without requiring existence, preserving logical consistency.

Checkpoint
What is the effect of restricting the subject to tasawwur in the second answer?
A) It makes the premise impossible to be true.
B) It allows the converse to be true without requiring the existence of the subject.
C) It proves that tasawwur cannot be acquired by kasb.
D) It invalidates the original objection.

Key Takeaway
Restricting the subject to tasawwur is the key move that saves the premises from contradiction, affirming the second answer as correct.

Third Answer: The Sufficiency of Direct Conversion
Commentator offers his own third answer about the absolute premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's third answer, which shows that the syllogism does not require contrapositive conversion because the original premise is already properly converted.

Core Idea
The original premise is sufficient; no need for contrapositive.

In this passage, the commentator offers his own third answer to the objection raised earlier. He argues that the premise used in the syllogism—'every perceived entity absolutely has its seeking impossible'—is already appropriately converted. Therefore, the syllogism stands without the need for contrapositive conversion. This concise but powerful logical move demonstrates that sometimes the simplest formulation is sufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the key logical terms: القضية, القياس, عكس الوجوه, عكس النقيض.
Notice that the commentator presents his answer as an alternative to previous ones.
2
Identify the premise and its conversion
The premise is 'كل مشعور به مطلقاً أي من جميع الوجوه يمتنع طلبه'. The conversion is 'عكس الوجوه يمتنع طلبه'. Understand how this conversion works in all respects.
This conversion is straightforward, not requiring contrapositive.
3
Understand the conclusion
The commentator concludes that contrapositive conversion is not needed; the syllogism is sound without it. Reflect on why this is the case.
The premise already covers all respects, so its direct conversion is sufficient.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this answer is noteworthy. It shows that the commentator believes the original formulation is robust enough to avoid more complex logical steps.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندي جواب آخر عن الاعتراض وهو أن القضية المأخوذة في القياس قولنا كل مشعور به مطلقاً أي من جميع الوجوه يمتنع طلبه، وعكس الوجوه يمتنع طلبه. فلا حاجة إلى عكس النقيض، بل القياس مستقيم بدونه.
And I have another answer to the objection, namely that the premise taken in the syllogism is our statement: 'Every perceived entity absolutely, i.e., from all respects, has its seeking impossible,' and its conversion in all respects is that seeking it is impossible. So there is no need for contrapositive conversion; rather the syllogism is sound without it.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents the commentator's own third answer to the objection. He argues that the premise used in the syllogism is 'Every perceived entity absolutely has its seeking impossible,' and its conversion is straightforward. Therefore, there is no need to resort to contrapositive conversion; the syllogism stands correctly without it.

Proof by Classification of Tasawwur into Perceived and Non-Perceived
Proof by division of tasawwur into perceived and non-perceived

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how logicians prove that seeking any concept (tasawwur) is impossible by dividing it into perceived and non-perceived categories and using logical conversions to ensure consistency.

Core Idea
Every concept is either already known or completely unknown—neither can be sought.

This lesson presents a tight logical proof from the classical kalam tradition. The argument divides every possible concept into two groups—those already perceived and those not perceived—and shows that both groups are impossible to seek. Then, anticipating objections, the proof uses logical conversions (contrapositive and simple conversion) to demonstrate that no contradiction arises. By working through this reasoning, you will see how precision in logic protects theological arguments from apparent paradoxes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the division of tasawwur
The proof begins by classifying every tasawwur as either perceived (already present in the mind) or non-perceived (not present). This exhaustive division sets up the argument.
This division covers all possibilities; nothing is left out.
2
See why both types make seeking impossible
If a concept is already perceived, you cannot seek it because you already have it. If it is not perceived, you cannot seek it because you have no idea what to look for. Thus, seeking any concept is impossible.
This is the core of the proof.
3
Follow the logical conversions for consistency
The author then applies contrapositive and simple conversion to the premises to show that they do not contradict each other. This ensures the proof is sound and free from logical flaws.
Pay attention to how the subject becomes more general in the converse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نحن نستدل هكذا التصور إما تصور مشعور به وإما تصور غير مشعور به وكل تصور مشعور به يمتنع طلبه وكل تصور غير مشعور به يمتنع طلبه، وحينئذ تنعكس الحملية الأولى بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا كل ما لا يمتنع طلبه فهو ليس تصوراً مشعوراً به وينعكس هذا العكس بالمستوي إلى قولنا بعض ما ليس تصوراً مشعوراً به لا يمتنع طلبه وهذا لا ينافي الحملية الثانية لأن موضوعه أعم من موضوعها، ألا ترى أن ما ليس تصوراً مشعوراً به جاز أن لا يكون تصوراً أصلاً، وأن يكون تصوراً غير مشعور به وقس على ذلك حال الحملية الثانية، فإن العكس المستوي لعكس نقيضها هو قولنا بعض ما ليس تصوراً غير مشعور به لا يمتنع طلبه، وموضوعه أعم من موضوع الحملية الأولى فلا منافاة بينهما.
We argue as follows: a concept (taṣawwur) is either a perceived concept or a non-perceived concept, and every perceived concept (kullu taṣawwurin mash‘ūrin bihi) has its seeking impossible (yamtani‘u ṭalabuhu), and every non-perceived concept has its seeking impossible. Then the first categorical premise converts by contrapositive to our statement: 'Everything whose seeking is not impossible is not a perceived concept,' and this converse converts by simple conversion to our statement: 'Some of what is not a perceived concept has its seeking not impossible.' This does not contradict the second categorical premise because its subject is more general than its subject. Do you not see that what is not a perceived concept may not be a concept at all, or may be a non-perceived concept? And analogize to that the situation of the second categorical premise, for the simple converse of its contrapositive is our statement: 'Some of what is not a non-perceived concept does not have its seeking impossible,' and its subject is more general than the subject of the first categorical premise, so there is no contradiction between them.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by division (taqsīm) to show that seeking any tasawwur (concept) is impossible. The proof divides every concept into either perceived (mash‘ur bihi) or non-perceived (ghayr mash‘ur bihi). Both categories are shown to be such that seeking them is impossible: perceived concepts cannot be sought because they are already present, and non-perceived concepts cannot be sought because they are not known. The proof then examines the logical conversions of the premises to show that no contradiction arises when the contrapositives and simple conversions are applied.

Key Terms
تصور
مشعور به
غير مشعور به
حملية
عكس النقيض
العكس المستوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular because it assumes the impossibility of seeking both categories and then uses logical conversions to justify it.
Correction: The proof is not circular. It first deduces the impossibility from the nature of perception, then uses logical conversions only to show that the two premises do not contradict each other, thereby strengthening the argument.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why does the simple converse of the contrapositive of the first premise not contradict the second premise?
A) Because the subject of that converse is more general than the subject of the second premise.
B) Because the second premise is false.
C) Because the conversion is invalid.
D) Because the first premise is not a universal affirmative.

Key Takeaway
This proof shows that the impossibility of seeking a concept is logically consistent by exhaustively dividing all concepts and using contraposition to eliminate apparent contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof concludes that seeking a concept is impossible because it is either already known or completely unknown. Yet you actively seek Islamic knowledge. How do you reconcile this logical impossibility with your own experience? What does this say about the role of divine guidance (hidāyah) in your learning? Identify one area where you are seeking something you think you don't know—could it be that you already know it but fail to act on it? Commit to acting on one known truth this week.
?
The author uses precise logical conversions to eliminate contradictions. Think of a contradiction in your own belief or practice that troubles you. What terms need clearer definition in your mind (like 'perceived' vs 'non-perceived') to resolve it? Write down the contradiction and the missing distinction, then seek clarity from the Qur’an or Sunnah today.
?
If seeking is impossible for both known and unknown, then how can you truly learn something new? This paradox forces you to rely on external teaching and revelation. In your daily life, do you truly humble yourself before the Quran and the Prophet’s example as the source of all new knowledge? Or do you assume you already know enough? Choose one verse or hadith that challenges your current understanding and study it deeply this week.

Second Proof: Impossibility of Acquiring an Essence by Itself or Its Parts
A proof that a mahiyya cannot be acquired via kasb by classifying and refuting possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for the impossibility of acquiring a conceptual concept (tasawwur) through kasb, specifically why defining an essence by itself or by its parts leads to circularity.

Core Idea
Every attempt to define an essence leads back to itself — circularity is unavoidable.

This lesson examines a key proof from the partisans of the Imam that a conceptual notion (tasawwur) cannot be acquired through kasb (acquisition) or nazar (investigation). The proof classifies all possible ways of knowing a mahiyya (essence) and shows each leads to impossibility. Here we focus on the first two divisions: knowing the essence by itself or by its parts. Both are shown to be circular.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice that the proof is structured as a classification of possibilities.
The passage is cut off after mentioning the second division; the third is incomplete.
2
Identify the three divisions
The passage lists three possible ways to know a mahiyya through kasb: by itself, by a part of it, or by something external. Mark these as the backbone of the proof.
The third division is not fully argued here, but it is mentioned.
3
Understand why the first division fails
The first option (by itself) leads to circularity: you would need to know it before knowing it, which is impossible. The phrase 'تقدّم الشيء على نفسه محال' is crucial.
4
Understand why the second division fails
The second option (by a part) also fails because 'all parts are itself' — so defining by parts is ultimately the same as defining by itself, thus circular.
5
Reflect on the implication for kasb
Ask yourself: If every attempt to define an essence is circular, can we ever acquire a concept through reasoning? This proof suggests no; concepts must be known through some other means.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني من متمسكي الإمام في امتناع كسبية التصور أن يقال: ( الماهية ) أي المفهوم التصوري ) أن عرفت ) وحصلت بالكسب والنظر ( فإما بنفسها أو بجزئها أو بالخارج منها سواء كان خارجاً بتمامه أو ببعضه ( والأقسام) بأسرها ( باطلة أما الأول فلأنه يستلزم معرفتها قبل معرفتها لأن معرفة المعرف الموصل متقدمة على المعرف الموصل إليه، وتقدم الشيء على نفسه محال بديهة ( وأما الثاني فلأن جميع الأجزاء نفسها فلا يجوز تعريف الماهية بجميع أجزائها لأنه
The second proof from the partisans of the Imām regarding the impossibility of the acquirability (kasbiyya) of a concept (taṣawwur) is that it is said: The essence (al-māhiyya) – i.e., the conceptual notion (al-mafhūm al-taṣawwurī) – when it is known and obtained by acquisition (kasb) and investigation (naẓar), then either by itself (bi-nafsihā), or by a part of it (bi-juz‘ihā), or by something external to it (bi-l-khāriji minhā), whether that external is entirely or partially. All the divisions are invalid. As for the first, because it necessitates knowing it before knowing it, since knowledge of the means of access (al-muwaṣṣil) precedes knowledge of the accessed (al-muwaṣṣal ilayhi), and the self's priority over itself is an impossibility by intuition (muḥāl badīhatan). As for the second, because all parts are itself (nafsuhā), so it is not permissible to define the essence by all its parts, because...
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the impossibility of acquiring a concept (tasawwur) through kasb (acquisition). It is structured as a classification of possible ways to know a mahiyya (essence) through kasb: either by itself, by a part of it, or by something external. Each possibility is shown to be invalid. The first option (by itself) leads to circularity: knowledge of the means of access must precede knowledge of the accessed, which would require knowing the thing before itself. The second option (by a part) is also impossible because the parts are identical to the whole; defining an essence by its parts is circular. The third option (by something external) is not fully discussed in the surviving text but is implied to be invalid as well.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that defining an essence by its parts avoids circularity because parts are distinct from the whole.
Correction: The argument shows that the parts are not truly distinct from the whole; they are identical to it ('جميع الأجزاء نفسها'), so defining by parts is still circular.

Checkpoint
Why is the first option (knowing the mahiyya by itself) impossible according to the proof?
A) Because the mahiyya is unknowable.
B) Because it requires prior knowledge of itself, which is circular.
C) Because the mahiyya has no parts.
D) Because kasb cannot produce certainty.

Key Takeaway
Any attempt to define a simple essence through reasoning leads to circularity, proving that conceptual concepts cannot be acquired through kasb.

?
This argument shows that some knowledge must be immediate (badīhī) and not acquired. Where in your own life have you accepted a definition or concept as 'just obvious' without examining it? Are you willing to submit your intellect to the possibility that ultimate realities are beyond acquisition?

Two Problems of Defining an Essence by a Part
Al-Iji enumerates two logical problems and offers a corrected formulation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two logical problems that arise when defining an essence by one of its parts, and how al-Iji resolves them.

Core Idea
Defining an essence by a part leads to circularity or external definition.

Al-Iji presents a subtle logical critique: if you try to define an entire essence using only a part of it, you run into two unavoidable problems. Either the part defines itself (circular) or it must rely on something outside the part (external). He then clarifies that these problems only arise if the part is taken as the whole. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parenthetical expressions which indicate alternative phrasings.
2
Identify the two problems
The passage presents two harmful consequences: (1) the part defines itself, leading to circularity; (2) the part defines something else, requiring an external definition. Find where each is mentioned.
3
Understand the resolution
Al-Iji states that these problems only follow if the part is the whole, which is prohibited. The proper formulation is that if a part defines the whole, it must define a part of it, which leads to either self-definition or external definition. Note that he rejects the original phrasing and offers a corrected one.
This shows the importance of precise wording in logical arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف للشيء بنفسه (والبعض) من أجزاء الماهية (إن عرفها وأنها لا تعرف) بالتخفيف من المعرفة (إلا بمعرفة جميع الأجزاء عرف ذلك البعض (نفسه وقد أبطل والخارج أي وعرف الجزء الخارج هو منه (وسيبطل) وهذان المحذوران إنما يلزمان معاً إذا كان ذلك البعض معرفاً، لكنه الماهية وهو ممنوع، فالأولى أن يقال: والبعض إن عرفها فلا بد أن يعرف جزءاً منها، فذلك الجزء إما نفسه فيكون معرفاً لنفسه، وإما غيره فيلزم التعريف بالخارج، لأن كل جزء خارج عما يقابله من الأجزاء
A definition of a thing by itself (and some) of the parts of the essence (if it defines it and that it is not known) with lightening from knowledge (except by knowledge of all the parts, that part is known (itself and it has been nullified and the external i.e. when the external part is known it is from it (and it will be nullified) and these two harmful consequences only follow together if that part is a definer, but it is the essence and that is prohibited, so it is better to say: and a part if it defines it, then it must define a part of it, so that part is either itself, so it is a definer of itself, or other, so definition by the external is necessary, because every part is external to what corresponds to it from the parts.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage defines the two problems that arise if one attempts to define an essence by some of its parts. First, if a part defines the whole, then it must define a part of the whole. That part could be itself, leading to self-definition, which is circular. Or it could be other than itself, which would require a definition by something external, which is also problematic. However, the author points out that these problems only occur if the part is the whole, which is not the case. So the proper way to state the argument is: if a part defines the whole, it must define a part of it. That part is either the same as itself (self-definition) or different (definition by external). The passage concludes that every part is external to its counterpart.

Key Terms
تعريف
الماهية
الجزء
الخارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a part can define the whole without any logical flaw, simply by referring to the whole.
Correction: The passage shows that such a definition inevitably leads to either circular self-definition or reliance on an external part, both of which are problematic.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a part of an essence defines the whole, what are the two possible outcomes for the part that the defining part must know?
A) It must be either the same as the defining part or different from it.
B) It must be either the whole or nothing.
C) It must be either prior or posterior.
D) It must be either intrinsic or extrinsic.

Key Takeaway
Defining an essence by a part inevitably leads to either circularity or reliance on an external definition, exposing the flaw in such a definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This passage shows that every part is external to every other part. In your own understanding of your character, do you define yourself by only one trait? How does that lead to an incomplete or even mistaken self-definition?

The Third Problem of Defining by External and Its Response
Al-Iji presents the third problem, a response, and a counter-objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third problem of definition by an external—the circularity and impossibility—and the attempted answer by the author of Naqd al-Muhassal and the counter-objection.

Core Idea
Defining by external leads to circularity unless parts are prior, but the answer itself is problematic.

Al-Iji presents the third major objection to defining a quiddity by an external thing: to do so requires comprehensive specification which demands prior knowledge, creating a vicious circle. He then reports a later scholar's attempt to solve this by appealing to the priority of parts, and finally offers a counter-objection. This passage is crucial for understanding the limits of definition in logic and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once without analyzing. Notice the structure: problem → answer → counter-objection.
The passage is cut off at the end, but the main argument is present.
2
Understand the circularity problem
The third problem is that definition by external requires comprehensive specification, which depends on knowing the essence beforehand, creating a circle. Also, knowing all other things in detail is impossible.
3
Analyze the later scholar's answer
The author of Naqd al-Muhassal argues that all parts of the essence are prior to it, so the whole (parts set) is not the essence, thus defining by all parts avoids circularity.
4
Grasp the counter-objection
Al-Iji objects via opposition: if the essence is other than all parts, then either it is with the parts or not—the argument is cut off but the intent is to show the answer fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث: فلأن الخارج لا يعرف الماهية إلا إذا كان شاملاً لأفرادها دون شيء مما عداها ليكون مميزاً لها عن جميع ما سواها، والعلم بذلك الاختصاص الشمولي يتوقف على تصورها، وذلك دور لتوقف تصور الماهية حينئذ على تعريف الخارج إياها، وتوقف تعريفه إياها على العلم بذلك الاختصاص المتوقف على تصورها، وتصور ما عداها مفصلاً، وأنه محال لاستحالة إحاطة الذهن بما لا يتناهى تفصيلاً. وأجاب عنه بعض المتأخرين يعني صاحب نقد المحصل بأن جميع أجزاء الماهية ليس نفسها إذ كل واحد من أجزائها مقدم عليها بالذات فكذا الكل يكون مقدماً عليها، فلا يكون نفسها لامتناع تقدم الشيء على نفسه، فجاز تعريفها بجميع أجزائها. قلنا في دفع هذا الجواب بطريق المعارضة: الماهية لو كانت غير جميع الأجزاء فإما معها أي فإما أن يكون تحصل الماهية مع الأجزاء، وإذ ليست تلك
As for the third: because the external does not define the essence unless it includes its individuals without anything other than it to be distinguished from all besides it, and knowledge of that inclusive specification depends on its conception, and that is a circle because the conception of the essence then depends on the external defining it, and its definition by the external depends on knowledge of that specification dependent on its conception, and the detailed conception of all besides it, and that is impossible because the mind cannot encompass the infinite in detail. And a later scholar, namely the author of Naqd al-Muhassal, answered that all the parts of the essence are not itself, since each of its parts is prior to it by essence, so likewise the whole is prior to it, so it is not itself because a thing cannot be prior to itself, so it is permissible to define it by all its parts. We said in refuting this answer by way of opposition: if the essence were other than all the parts, then it would either be with them, i.e., the essence would occur with the parts, and it is not that.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents the third problem of definition by an external: to define an essence by an external, that external must be comprehensive, covering all individuals and distinguishing the essence from all others. But knowing that comprehensive specification requires prior knowledge of the essence, resulting in circularity. Moreover, detailed knowledge of all other things is impossible. Then the later scholar (author of Naqd al-Muhassal) answers by arguing that the whole is not the same as its parts because each part is prior by essence, so the totality is prior to the essence, and thus the essence is not identical to its parts; therefore definition by all parts is permissible. Finally, the author (via "we said") objects via opposition: if the essence is different from all parts, then it must be either together with the parts or not—the argument continues but is cut off.

تعريف بالخارج
الاختصاص الشمولي
دور
محال
جميع الأجزاء
تقدم بالذات
معارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a comprehensive external definition is unproblematic and straightforward.
Correction: This passage shows that such definition leads to circularity and impossibility, and even the proposed solution via parts-priority is challenged.

Checkpoint

A) It requires knowing all parts of the essence in detail.
B) It requires knowing all other things in detail, which is infinite.
C) It requires knowing the external itself.
D) It requires the external to be a part of the essence.

Key Takeaway
The third problem shows that definition by external leads to circularity and impossibility, and the attempted resolution by parts-priority is challenged by a counter-objection.

The Circularity Problem in Defining Essence by Parts and Its Solution
Al-Iji presents the circularity and infinity argument against definition by all parts, then the reply that parts are not the mahiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical problem of defining an essence by all its parts and how later scholars resolved it by distinguishing parts from the essence itself.

Core Idea
Defining by all parts traps knowledge in a circle—until we see that parts are not the whole.

In his discussion of definition, Al-Iji presents a powerful objection: if you define something by listing all its parts, you need to already know what it is to identify those parts. This leads to a vicious circle and impossible infinite knowledge. A later scholar, author of Naqd al-Muhassal, offers a way out by showing that the parts are essentially prior to the whole, so they cannot be identical with what they define. Let's see the argument and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the problem of definition by all parts
Al-Iji explains that defining a mahiyya by all its parts to distinguish it from everything else requires prior knowledge of that specificity, creating a circular dependency. Read the passage carefully.
Notice the terms 'dawr' (vicious circle) and 'tasalsul' (infinite regress).
2
Understand the vicious circle and infinity argument
The definer's knowledge depends on conceptualizing the mahiyya and everything else, which is both circular and impossible due to infinite details. Follow the logical steps.
The passage says it's impossible for the mind to encompass infinite things in detail.
3
Examine the later scholar's reply
The reply argues that parts are not the mahiyya because each part is prior to the whole, and the whole is prior to the mahiyya, so they cannot be identical. Hence defining by all parts is permissible.
The key is that parts are essentially prior, so they are distinct from the mahiyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الرابع : المذاهب الضعيفة في هذه المسألة
مما عداها ليكون مميزاً لها عن جميع ما سواها والعلم بذلك الاختصاص الشمولي يتوقف على تصورها وأنه دور لتوقف تصور الماهية حينئذ على تعريف الخارج إياها، وتوقف تعريفه إياها على العلم بذلك الاختصاص المتوقف على تصورها وتصور ما عداها مفصلاً، وأنه محال لاستحالة إحاطة الذهن بمالا يتناهى تفصيلاً وأجاب عنه بعض المتأخرين يعني صاحب نقد المحصل بأن جميع أجزاء الماهية ليس نفسها إذ كل واحد من أجزائها مقدم عليها بالذات فكذا الكل يكون مقدماً عليها، فلا يكون نفسها لامتناع تقدم الشيء على نفسه، فجاز تعريفها بجميع أجزائها
The third observatory - the fourth aim: the weak schools in this issue. From what excludes it, so that it distinguishes it from everything other than it. And knowledge of that comprehensive specificity depends on its conceptualization, and that is a vicious circle, because the conceptualization of the essence (mahiyya) at that point depends on the external (the definer) defining it, and the definer's defining it depends on knowledge of that specificity which depends on its conceptualization and the detailed conceptualization of what is other than it. And that is impossible because of the impossibility of the mind encompassing what is infinite in detail. And some later scholars, namely the author of Naqd al-Muhassal, answered that all the parts of the essence are not the essence itself, since each one of its parts is prior to it in essence, so likewise the whole is prior to it, so it is not itself because the priority of a thing over itself is impossible, so defining it by all its parts is permissible.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents a problem and a reply. The problem is that defining an essence (mahiyya) by all its parts, in order to distinguish it from everything else, requires prior knowledge of that comprehensive specificity. This leads to a vicious circle (dawr): conceptualizing the mahiyya depends on an external definition, which depends on the specificity, which depends on conceptualization and detailed knowledge of everything else. Moreover, detailed knowledge of infinite things is impossible. The reply from the author of Naqd al-Muhassal argues that all parts are not the mahiyya itself, because each part is essentially prior to the whole, and the whole is prior to the mahiyya, so the whole cannot be identical to the mahiyya (since that would require self-priority). Therefore, defining the mahiyya by all its parts is valid.

Key Terms
dawr
tasalsul
mahiyya
juz'
kull

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that the whole is nothing more than the sum of its parts, so defining by parts is the same as defining the essence itself.
Correction: The passage shows that parts are essentially prior to the essence, so they cannot be identical to it. Defining by parts is valid precisely because they are not the essence.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given for the impossibility of definition by all parts?
A) The parts are not distinct from the whole
B) It requires prior knowledge of infinite things and leads to a vicious circle
C) The whole cannot be defined by its parts
D) The definer cannot know the essence

Key Takeaway
Defining an essence by its parts is only logically valid if the parts are not identical to the essence, as argued by later kalam scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a core belief you hold about yourself, like 'I am a good Muslim.' Do you define that belief by listing actions (parts) such as praying, fasting, charity? According to this lesson, those actions are parts, not your essence. Have you ever mistaken your actions for your identity? How would your self-understanding change if you saw your actions as separate from your core essence before Allah? Commit to one specific action you will take to realign your identity with your essence rather than your parts.
?
The vicious circle arises when knowledge depends on itself. In your faith, is there any belief that you hold simply because you always have, without examining its foundation? Are you caught in a 'dawr' of comfort? This lesson challenges you to break the circle by distinguishing the essence of your belief from its accidental parts. The Essence and Its Parts: The Objection (Mu'aarada)
The commentator presents a dilemma to refute the claim that the mahiyya is distinct from all parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that arises if the essence (mahiyya) is considered distinct from all its parts, and how the objection (mu'aarada) refutes the claim that the parts are all there is to the essence.

Core Idea
If the essence is not the same as all its parts, then it must be either inside or outside – both impossible.

The commentator now presents a direct objection (mu'aarada) to the earlier reply. The argument is a dilemma: if the mahiyya is other than all its parts, then either it exists with the parts or without them, leading to contradictions. Understanding this dilemma is crucial for grasping the logical foundations of definition in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the commentator's own formulation come through before you interpret.
Notice the dilemma structure: two horns lead to contradiction.
2
Identify the two horns
Find the two possible relations between the mahiyya and the parts: either the mahiyya is realized with the parts (but then the parts are not 'all') or without them (but then they are not parts).
Each horn leads to an impossibility.
3
Examine the clearer formulation
The commentator restates the dilemma more clearly: either the mahiyya is included in 'all parts' so it is not 'all', or it is external so the parts are not parts.
This restatement highlights the force of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا في دفع هذا الجواب بطريق المعارضة الماهية لو كانت غير جميع الأجزاء فإما معها أي فإما أن يكون تحصل الماهية مع الأجزاء، وإذ ليست تلك الأجزاء تمامها فلا بد هناك من أمر آخر معتبر في ذاتها فلا تكون جميع الأجزاء جميعاً هذا خلف أو دونها أي أو يكون تحصلها بدون الأجزاء وقطع النظر عنها فلا تكون أجزاء لاستحالة تحصل الماهية بدون أجزائها، والأظهر في العبارة أن يقال: لو لم يكن جميع الأجزاء نفس الماهية فإما أن يكون داخلاً فيها فلا يكون جميعاً أو خارجاً عنها فلا يكون أجزاء وقلنا في دفعه بطريق المناقضة لا يلزم من تقدم كل من الأجزاء على الماهية تقدم الكل عليها فإن الكل المجموعي وكل واحد قد يتخالفان في الأحكام، فإن كل إنسان تسعه هذه الدار التي لا تسع كلهم، وكل العسكر يهزم العدو الذي لا يهزمه كل واحد منهم، بل نقول: كل واحد الأجزاء جزء من الكل المجموعي الذي ليس جزءاً لنفسه، ثم إنه أيد هذه المناقضة بقوله وإلا أي وإن لم يصح ما ذكرناه من أنه ليس يلزم من تقدم كل واحد على شيء تقدم الكل عليه تقدم الكل أي كل الأجزاء على نفسه لأن كل واحد منها متقدم على كلها كتقدمه على الماهية بعينه ويمكن أن يجعل هذا نقضاً إجمالياً كما لا يخفى فإن أراد هذا المجيب بجميع الأجزاء جميعها مطلقاً بحيث يتناول المادية والصورية معاً، فدفع جوابه ما قدمناه وإن أراد به الأجزاء المادية فقط لم يكن ما أراده أعني الأجزاء المادية وحدها جميعاً حقيقة بل بعضاً داخلاً في القسم الثاني ولا كافية في معرفة كنه الماهية فلا يكون التعريف بها حداً تاماً، والكلام فيه
We say in refuting this answer by way of objection (mu'aarada): The essence (mahiyya) if it were other than all the parts, then either (it is) with them, i.e., the essence is realized with the parts, while those parts are not the entirety of it, so there must be another consideration in its essence, thus all the parts are not 'all' – this is a contradiction – or without them, i.e., the essence is realized without the parts and disregarding them, then they would not be parts because of the impossibility of the essence being realized without its parts. The more apparent in expression is to say: If all the parts are not the essence itself, then either it is included in them (so it is not 'all') or it is external to them (so they are not parts). And we say in refuting it by way of contradiction (munaqada): The priority of each of the parts over the essence does not necessitate the priority of the whole over it, for the collective whole (kull majmu'i) and each individual may differ in their properties. For instance, a house may accommodate every individual human but not all of them together, and an army may defeat an enemy that no single soldier can defeat. Rather, we say: Each part is a part of the collective whole, which is not a part of itself. Then he supported this contradiction by saying: 'Otherwise' (i.e., if what we mentioned is not valid), then the priority of each over something would necessitate the priority of the whole over that same thing, leading to the priority of the whole over itself, because each part is prior to the whole just as it is prior to the essence itself. And this can be considered an overall objection (naqd ijmaili), as is clear. If this respondent (the later scholar) means by 'all the parts' all of them absolutely, including material and formal parts, then our previous refutation applies. If he means only the material parts, then what he intended (i.e., the material parts alone) are not in reality 'all' but rather some, falling under the second category, and they are not sufficient for knowing the true nature (kunn) of the essence, so definition by them would not be a perfect definition (hadd tam), and the discussion continues.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage refutes the earlier reply by way of mu'aarada (objection). It first presents a dilemma: if the mahiyya is distinct from all parts, either it exists with the parts (implying it needs an extra element, contradicting the claim that the parts are all) or it exists without them (contradicting them being parts). A clearer formulation is that the mahiyya is either included in all parts (so not all) or external (so the parts are not parts). Then it uses munaqada (contradiction) to argue that the priority of each part over the mahiyya does not imply the priority of the whole over it, giving examples of a house and an army where collective properties differ from individual ones. It further argues that if the reply is accepted, it leads to the whole being prior to itself. Finally, it distinguishes between absolute 'all parts' (including formal) and mere material parts: if absolute, the refutation stands; if only material, then it's not truly 'all' and insufficient for a proper definition.

Key Terms
mu'aarada (objection)
mahiyya (essence)
juz' (part)
kull (all)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the outcome of the first horn of the dilemma?
A) The mahiyya is realized without parts.
B) The parts are not 'all' because something else is needed.
C) The parts become the whole.
D) The definition becomes perfect.

Key Takeaway
The objection (mu'aarada) demonstrates that the mahiyya cannot be something other than all its parts, because any alternative leads to logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe your identity is more than just your body and mind. But if your 'essence' were not identical to all your parts, where would it be—inside or outside? This dilemma leaves no escape. How does this force you to reconsider what truly constitutes your being before Allah?
?
The objection (mu'aarada) reveals that assuming a separate essence leads to logical absurdity. Are there areas of your faith where you hold onto seemingly 'convenient' beliefs without checking for hidden contradictions? Will you let the light of reasoning purify your understanding?
?
If the mahiyya cannot be separated from its parts, then your actions are not external to your character; they are part of your essence. Does your daily life reflect this unity, or do you live as if your deeds are removable extras? What change must you make today to align your actions with your true self?

The Contradiction (Munaqada): Priority of Parts and the Collective Whole
The commentator uses contradiction and examples to show that collective properties differ from individual ones, and distinguishes material and formal parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the priority of each part over the essence does not entail the priority of the whole over it, using examples and the concept of the collective whole (kull majmu'i).

Core Idea
A collective whole can have properties that differ from those of its individual parts.

After the objection, the commentator employs a contradiction (munaqada) to further refute the claim. He argues that the priority of each part over the mahiyya does not transfer to the whole. The examples of a house and an army vividly illustrate this principle. The lesson also covers the final distinction between material and formal parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the munaqada argument
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the logical point: priority of each part does not imply priority of the whole. Then read the translation.
This argument directly attacks the reasoning of the earlier reply.
2
Analyze the examples
The examples of a house and an army show that what is true of each individual is not necessarily true of the collective. Consider how these examples support the argument.
A house can hold each person but not all together; an army can defeat an enemy no single soldier can.
3
Understand the final distinction
The commentator distinguishes between 'all parts' including formal parts versus just material parts. If the respondent means only material parts, that is not truly 'all' and insufficient for a perfect definition.
This ties back to the definition of the mahiyya.

We say in refuting this answer by way of objection (mu'aarada): The essence (mahiyya) if it were other than all the parts, then either (it is) with them, i.e., the essence is realized with the parts, while those parts are not the entirety of it, so there must be another consideration in its essence, thus all the parts are not 'all' – this is a contradiction – or without them, i.e., the essence is realized without the parts and disregarding them, then they would not be parts because of the impossibility of the essence being realized without its parts. The more apparent in expression is to say: If all the parts are not the essence itself, then either it is included in them (so it is not 'all') or it is external to them (so they are not parts). And we say in refuting it by way of contradiction (munaqada): The priority of each of the parts over the essence does not necessitate the priority of the whole over it, for the collective whole (kull majmu'i) and each individual may differ in their properties. For instance, a house may accommodate every individual human but not all of them together, and an army may defeat an enemy that no single soldier can defeat. Rather, we say: Each part is a part of the collective whole, which is not a part of itself. Then he supported this contradiction by saying: 'Otherwise' (i.e., if what we mentioned is not valid), then the priority of each over something would necessitate the priority of the whole over that same thing, leading to the priority of the whole over itself, because each part is prior to the whole just as it is prior to the essence itself. Commentary
This passage refutes the earlier reply by way of mu'aarada (objection). It first presents a dilemma: if the mahiyya is distinct from all parts, either it exists with the parts (implying it needs an extra element, contradicting the claim that the parts are all) or it exists without them (contradicting them being parts). Key Terms
munaqada (contradiction)
kull majmu'i (collective whole)
naqd ijmaili (overall objection)
hadd tam (perfect definition)
juz' maddi (material part)
juz' suri (formal part)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the priority of each part automatically entails the priority of the whole.
Correction: The collective whole (kull majmu'i) has properties distinct from its parts, as shown by the house and army examples.

Checkpoint
What do the examples of the house and army illustrate?
A) The whole cannot hold all individuals.

The contradiction (munaqada) establishes that the priority of parts does not transfer to the whole; a collective whole has its own distinct logical status.

?

?

?
The argument uses the example of an army: individual soldiers cannot defeat the enemy, but the army can. Will you seek the support of the whole (the Muslim community) more actively from now on?

Definition of the Heedless Exempt from Obligation
Defines two types of heedless exempt from obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of heedless persons who are exempt from religious obligation by consensus or by scholarly opinion.

Core Idea
Heedlessness of the concept excuses; heedlessness of belief does not.

In Islamic theology, the concept of taklif (obligation) hinges on the subject's ability to understand the address. This passage defines two categories of heedless persons and clarifies that mere lack of belief does not remove obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the core definition
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the two categories of the heedless.
Focus on the phrases 'لا يفهم الخطاب أصلاً' and 'أو يفهم ذلك ولكن لم يقل له إنك مكلف'.
2
Identify the consensus claim
Note that the first type is exempt by agreement (اتفاقاً) and the second only according to the author's school (عندنا).
This shows the consensus applies only to the first.
3
Distinguish conception from belief
The passage emphasizes that heedlessness of tasdiq (belief) does not excuse; otherwise unbelievers would not be obligated.
Understand the difference between tasawwur and tasdiq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب أن الغافل الذي لا يجوز تكليفه إجماعاً من لا يفهم الخطاب أصلاً كالصبي والمجنون أو يفهم ذلك ولكن لم يقل له إنك مكلف كالذي لم تبلغه دعوة نبي قطعا فإن هذين غافلان عن تصور التكليف بالتنبيه عليه فلا تكليف على الأول اتفاقاً، ولا على الثاني عندنا لا من لا يعلم أنه مكلف مع أنه خوطب بكونه مكلفاً حال ما كان الثاني فاهما، فإنه غافل عن التصديق بالتكليف لا عن تصوره، وذلك لا يمنع من تكليفه وإلا لم يكن الكفار مكلفين إذ ليسوا مصدقين بالتكليف. قوله : ( أن الغافل (الخ يعني أن الغافل الذي حكم عليه بأنه لا يجوز تكليفه إجماعاً له، فرد أن أحدهما متفق عليه والآخر مختل فيه والإجماع على الحكم باعتبار عنوان الغافل لا ينافي الاختلاف في صدقه على بعض الأشياء، فلا ينافي قيد الإجماع بقوله، ولا على الثاني عندنا وما قيل : إن المراد أن الغافل الذي لا يجوز تكليفه إجماعاً لا يخرج عن أحد المذكورين، لأن كلاً منهما لا يجوز تكليفه إجماعاً حتى ينافيه فلا يخفى ركاكته إذ المحكوم عليه بعدم الجواز إجماعاً ليس إلا الواحد المعين، فلا فائدة لضم النوع الآخر إليه، والحكم على سبيل الإبهام. قوله : ( فإنه غافل الخ) أشار بذلك إلى أن الجواب بالترديد وحاصله أنه إن أريد بالعلم في قوله : لا يعلم التكليف التصور نمنع الصغرى أعني قوله : لأن من لا يعلم هذه الأمور لا يعلم التكليف، لأن تصور التكليف لا يتوقف على التصديق بالأمور المذكورة وإن أريد به التصديق نمنع الكبرى أعني قوله : وإذا لم يعلم التكليف أي لم يصدق به كان غافلاً، فإن الغافل من لا يفهمه أو لم يقل له : إنك مكلف وإن أريد به التصديق اليقيني كما هو اللازم من الحد المختار، فدائرة البحث أوسع لجواز أن يكون ظانا بالتكليف أو مقلداً به، وإنما لم يقيد الشارح التصديق باليقيني لأن الغافل في الاصطلاح هو الغافل عن التصور.
And the answer is that the heedless whose obligation is not permissible by consensus is one who does not understand the address at all, like a child or a madman, or one who understands it but has not been told that he is obligated, like one whom a prophet’s call has absolutely not reached. For these two are heedless of the conception of obligation by way of attention to it; so there is no obligation upon the first by agreement, nor upon the second according to us. But [the heedless who is exempt is] not one who does not know that he is obligated despite being addressed as obligated while he is understanding; for such a one is heedless of the belief in obligation, not of its conception, and that does not prevent his obligation. Otherwise, the unbelievers would not be obligated, since they are not believers in obligation. His statement: (that the heedless…) means that the heedless who is judged that his obligation is not permissible by consensus has two individuals: one agreed upon and the other disputed, and the consensus on the judgment by virtue of the title “heedless” does not negate the disagreement about its applicability to some things, so it does not negate the restriction of consensus by his statement: “nor upon the second according to us.” And what is said: that the intended meaning is that the heedless whose obligation is not permissible by consensus does not fall outside one of the two mentioned, because each of them is not permissible to obligate by consensus until it contradicts, is not hidden in its weakness, because the one judged as not permissible by consensus is only the specific one, so there is no benefit in joining the other type to it and judging vaguely. His statement: (for he is heedless…) he indicated by that that the answer is by way of disjunction, and its summary is that if by “knowledge” in his statement “he does not know the obligation” is meant conception, we deny the minor premise, namely his statement: “because whoever does not know these things does not know the obligation,” because the conception of obligation does not depend on belief in the mentioned things. And if by it is meant belief, we deny the major premise, namely his statement: “and when he does not know the obligation, i.e., does not believe in it, he is heedless,” for the heedless is one who does not understand it or has not been told: “you are obligated.” And if by it is meant certain belief, as is necessary from the chosen definition, then the scope of inquiry is wider, because it is possible that he is assuming the obligation or following it blindly. And the commentator did not restrict belief to certain because the heedless in terminology is the one heedless of conception.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage defines who qualifies as 'al-ghafil' (the heedless) whose obligation is unanimously impermissible. The author distinguishes two types: one who does not understand the address at all (like a child or madman), and one who understands but has never been informed of obligation (like someone unreached by a prophet). Both are heedless of the very concept of obligation. The first is exempt by consensus; the second is exempt according to the author's school. However, the person who does understand the address and has been told he is obligated, yet does not believe in the obligation, is not truly heedless of the concept; he is only heedless of belief (tasdiq). This does not remove his obligation, otherwise unbelievers would not be obligated. The commentary explains that the objection based on consensus does not undermine the distinction: consensus applies only to the first type, not the second. The commentator also resolves a potential equivocation in the term 'knowledge': if it means conception, the minor premise is denied; if it means belief, the major premise is denied; if it means certain belief, the argument does not apply to those with mere opinion or taqlid. The key nuance is that the technical term 'ghafil' in kalam refers to heedlessness of conception, not of belief.

Key Terms
الغافل
التكليف
التصور
التصديق
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any lack of knowledge of obligation removes responsibility.
Correction: Only heedlessness of the concept (tasawwur) removes obligation; heedlessness of belief (tasdiq) does not.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following is exempt from obligation by consensus?
A) A person who understands the address but does not believe in the obligation.
B) A person who has never been told that he is obligated.
C) A person who does not understand the address at all, like an infant.
D) A person who understands the address but doubts it.

Key Takeaway
Heedlessness of the concept of obligation excuses; heedlessness of belief does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there religious obligations you know about but struggle to believe truly apply to you? What does that reveal about the state of your belief (tasdiq) versus your conception (tasawwur)? Will you ask Allah tonight to give you sincere belief in His commands?
?
This lesson shows that even someone who has never heard of Islam is not automatically excused from obligation—rather, they are excused only from the concept. Have you ever assumed ignorance absolves you without examining whether you actually understood the concept? Will you today seek knowledge of the obligations you have been neglecting?
?
If the unbelievers are still obligated despite not believing in obligation, how seriously should you take the obligations you already know? What is the one obligation you most often defer, and what step will you take right now to fulfill it?

Refutation of the Knowledge Condition: Tafsil
Refutes the condition of knowledge by disjunction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator resolves the objection regarding the requirement of knowledge for obligation by distinguishing conception, belief, and certain belief.

Core Idea
The term 'knowledge' in the objection is equivocal; each meaning leads to a different denial.

An objection is raised: if the heedless is defined as one who does not know the obligation, then why exempt the second type? The commentator answers by disambiguating 'knowledge' into conception, belief, and certain belief, showing that the objection fails in each case.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection claims that the definition of the heedless should exclude the second type if 'knowledge' is required.
The objector uses 'knowledge' ambiguously.
2
Follow the disjunction
The commentator analyzes three possible meanings of 'knowledge': conception, belief, and certain belief.
For each, either the minor or major premise is denied.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The objection fails because heedlessness of conception is the only relevant factor; the technical term 'ghafil' refers to that.
The commentator's answer is comprehensive.

Source Text

والجواب أن الغافل الذي لا يجوز تكليفه إجماعاً من لا يفهم الخطاب أصلاً كالصبي والمجنون أو يفهم ذلك ولكن لم يقل له إنك مكلف كالذي لم تبلغه دعوة نبي قطعا فإن هذين غافلان عن تصور التكليف بالتنبيه عليه فلا تكليف على الأول اتفاقاً، ولا على الثاني عندنا لا من لا يعلم أنه مكلف مع أنه خوطب بكونه مكلفاً حال ما كان الثاني فاهما، فإنه غافل عن التصديق بالتكليف لا عن تصوره، وذلك لا يمنع من تكليفه وإلا لم يكن الكفار مكلفين إذ ليسوا مصدقين بالتكليف. قوله : ( أن الغافل (الخ يعني أن الغافل الذي حكم عليه بأنه لا يجوز تكليفه إجماعاً له، فرد أن أحدهما متفق عليه والآخر مختل فيه والإجماع على الحكم باعتبار عنوان الغافل لا ينافي الاختلاف في صدقه على بعض الأشياء، فلا ينافي قيد الإجماع بقوله، ولا على الثاني عندنا وما قيل : إن المراد أن الغافل الذي لا يجوز تكليفه إجماعاً لا يخرج عن أحد المذكورين، لأن كلاً منهما لا يجوز تكليفه إجماعاً حتى ينافيه فلا يخفى ركاكته إذ المحكوم عليه بعدم الجواز إجماعاً ليس إلا الواحد المعين، فلا فائدة لضم النوع الآخر إليه، والحكم على سبيل الإبهام. قوله : ( فإنه غافل الخ) أشار بذلك إلى أن الجواب بالترديد وحاصله أنه إن أريد بالعلم في قوله : لا يعلم التكليف التصور نمنع الصغرى أعني قوله : لأن من لا يعلم هذه الأمور لا يعلم التكليف، لأن تصور التكليف لا يتوقف على التصديق بالأمور المذكورة وإن أريد به التصديق نمنع الكبرى أعني قوله : وإذا لم يعلم التكليف أي لم يصدق به كان غافلاً، فإن الغافل من لا يفهمه أو لم يقل له : إنك مكلف وإن أريد به التصديق اليقيني كما هو اللازم من الحد المختار، فدائرة البحث أوسع لجواز أن يكون ظانا بالتكليف أو مقلداً به، وإنما لم يقيد الشارح التصديق باليقيني لأن الغافل في الاصطلاح هو الغافل عن التصور.
And the answer is that the heedless whose obligation is not permissible by consensus is one who does not understand the address at all, like a child or a madman, or one who understands it but has not been told that he is obligated, like one whom a prophet’s call has absolutely not reached. For these two are heedless of the conception of obligation by way of attention to it; so there is no obligation upon the first by agreement, nor upon the second according to us. But [the heedless who is exempt is] not one who does not know that he is obligated despite being addressed as obligated while he is understanding; for such a one is heedless of the belief in obligation, not of its conception, and that does not prevent his obligation. Otherwise, the unbelievers would not be obligated, since they are not believers in obligation. His statement: (that the heedless…) means that the heedless who is judged that his obligation is not permissible by consensus has two individuals: one agreed upon and the other disputed, and the consensus on the judgment by virtue of the title “heedless” does not negate the disagreement about its applicability to some things, so it does not negate the restriction of consensus by his statement: “nor upon the second according to us.” And what is said: that the intended meaning is that the heedless whose obligation is not permissible by consensus does not fall outside one of the two mentioned, because each of them is not permissible to obligate by consensus until it contradicts, is not hidden in its weakness, because the one judged as not permissible by consensus is only the specific one, so there is no benefit in joining the other type to it and judging vaguely. His statement: (for he is heedless…) he indicated by that that the answer is by way of disjunction, and its summary is that if by “knowledge” in his statement “he does not know the obligation” is meant conception, we deny the minor premise, namely his statement: “because whoever does not know these things does not know the obligation,” because the conception of obligation does not depend on belief in the mentioned things. And if by it is meant belief, we deny the major premise, namely his statement: “and when he does not know the obligation, i.e., does not believe in it, he is heedless,” for the heedless is one who does not understand it or has not been told: “you are obligated.” And if by it is meant certain belief, as is necessary from the chosen definition, then the scope of inquiry is wider, because it is possible that he is assuming the obligation or following it blindly. And the commentator did not restrict belief to certain because the heedless in terminology is the one heedless of conception.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage defines who qualifies as 'al-ghafil' (the heedless) whose obligation is unanimously impermissible. The author distinguishes two types: one who does not understand the address at all (like a child or madman), and one who understands but has never been informed of obligation (like someone unreached by a prophet). Both are heedless of the very concept of obligation. The first is exempt by consensus; the second is exempt according to the author's school. However, the person who does understand the address and has been told he is obligated, yet does not believe in the obligation, is not truly heedless of the concept; he is only heedless of belief (tasdiq). This does not remove his obligation, otherwise unbelievers would not be obligated. The commentary explains that the objection based on consensus does not undermine the distinction: consensus applies only to the first type, not the second. The commentator also resolves a potential equivocation in the term 'knowledge': if it means conception, the minor premise is denied; if it means belief, the major premise is denied; if it means certain belief, the argument does not apply to those with mere opinion or taqlid. The key nuance is that the technical term 'ghafil' in kalam refers to heedlessness of conception, not of belief.

D) Neither premise

Key Takeaway
The objection against the definition of the heedless fails because the term 'knowledge' is ambiguous; proper disambiguation resolves it.

?

Circularity Argument Against Knowledge Condition
Proves that conditioning obligation on knowledge of it leads to circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why conditioning obligation on knowledge of the obligation leads to a vicious circle, and why alternative proposals like conjecture or taqlid fail to resolve it.

Core Idea
Obligation cannot depend on knowledge of itself without falling into circularity.

Theologians debated whether a person must know they are obligated before the obligation takes effect. Al-Jurjani presents a powerful rational argument: knowledge of an event is a shadow of the event itself, so conditioning obligation on prior knowledge would create a logical circle. This lesson unpacks that argument and examines the responses it provoked.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Note the key claim: 'knowledge is a shadow of the occurrence'.
The phrase 'ظل لوقوعه' is a classic expression of epistemological dependency.
2
Identify the circularity
Trace the reasoning: X (knowledge) depends on Y (obligation). If Y depends on X, then X depends on Y which depends on X, etc. That is the circle.
The commentator calls this the 'second proof'.
3
Consider objections and responses
The passage includes an objection by al-Jahiz and the author's rebuttal, plus further objections about conjecture, taqlid, and necessity. Think about whether these objections are persuasive.
The commentator dismisses al-Jahiz's objection as irrelevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأن العلم بوقوع التكليف موقوف على وقوعه فإن العلم بوقوع شيء ظل لوقوعه في نفسه فلو توقف وقوعه على العلم والتصديق به لزم الدور. قوله : ( عطف على ما تقدم الخ ) فهو دليل ثان لقوله لا من لا يعلم أنه مكلف، وحاصله أن اشتراطه يستلزم الدور، فالجواب بأن مراد الجاحظ أن الموقوف عليه لوقوع التكليف، يتوقف على وقوع التكليف فلا دور كلام لا مساس له أصلاً . يشير إليه بقوله ولأن العلم بوقوع التكليف إلى آخره فيرد أنه لم لا يكفي الظن أو التقليد ؟ وأيضاً الضرورة لا تستلزم الحصول، فيلزم أن لا يكلف من لم يحصل له ما يتوقف عليه التكليف، فإن قلت : إذا كان الموقوف عليه ضرورياً يكفي للتكليف التنبيه عليه، قلت : له عدم الإصغاء حينئذ فتأمل .
And because knowledge of the occurrence of obligation depends on its occurrence, for knowledge of the occurrence of a thing is a shadow of its occurrence in itself. So if its occurrence were dependent on knowledge and belief of it, circularity would follow. His statement: (it is coordinated with what preceded) so it is a second proof for his statement: “not one who does not know that he is obligated,” and its summary is that conditioning it entails circularity. So the answer that what al-Jahiz meant is that what the occurrence of obligation depends on is dependent on the occurrence of obligation, so there is no circularity, is speech that has no bearing at all. He indicates by his statement “and because knowledge of the occurrence of obligation” etc., and it is objected: why is conjecture or taqlid not sufficient? Also, necessity does not necessitate obtaining, so it would follow that one for whom what obligation depends on has not been obtained is not obligated. If you say: if what it depends on is necessary, then reminding is sufficient for obligation, I say: then he has no heed at that time, so reflect.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof against the condition that knowledge of obligation is required for obligation itself. The matn states: knowledge of the occurrence of obligation depends on the actual occurrence of obligation itself, because knowledge follows the known. If obligation's occurrence were conditioned on knowledge/belief of it, a vicious circle (dawr) would result. The commentary explains that this is a second proof (the first being the definition of the heedless). It refutes a potential objection attributed to al-Jahiz: that what obligation depends on is the belief in the possibility of obligation, which itself does not depend on actual obligation. The commentator dismisses this as irrelevant because the proof is about knowledge of the occurrence, not possibility. The commentary then raises further issues: why not suffice with conjecture or taqlid? Also, if the precondition is necessary, merely reminding might still fail if the person does not listen. The argument firmly establishes that obligation cannot be logically preceded by knowledge of itself.

العلم بوقوع التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the circularity argument can be easily avoided by redefining the precondition (e.g., as belief in possibility rather than actual knowledge).
Correction: The commentator shows that the proof specifically targets knowledge of the occurrence, not just any belief, so such redefinitions miss the point.

Checkpoint
What logical problem arises if obligation depends on knowledge of obligation?
A) Infinite regress.
B) Circularity (dawr).
C) Contradiction.
D) Incoherence.

Key Takeaway
Conditioning obligation on prior knowledge of itself leads to a logical circle, because knowledge of an occurring event depends on the event itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that conditioning obligation on prior knowledge leads to circularity. Think about a duty you have been postponing—whether toward Allah or others—because you keep saying 'I need to know more first.' How is that excuse actually a form of circular reasoning that keeps you from ever starting?
?
The commentator dismisses al-Jahiz's objection as irrelevant. Consider your own heart: Are there times when you redefine what is required of you to avoid the clear call of obligation? What would it mean to stop avoiding and simply respond to the obligation as it is?
?
The passage ends with 'then he has no heed at that time, so reflect.' When has your own lack of heed—your turning away from a reminder—been the real barrier, not a lack of knowledge? What will you do today to break that pattern?

Intuitive Knowledge with Implicit Proofs
The commentator answers the objection that the three necessary propositions are theoretical, not intuitive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the three necessary propositions remain intuitive (badīhiyya) even though they are supported by proofs, and why the presence of proofs does not make them theoretical.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge can have proofs that are implicitly known.

The objector claims that if the three necessary propositions require precise proofs, they cannot be intuitive. The commentator responds by pointing out that these proofs are implicitly observed by all rational people, even if not articulated. The meaning matters more than wording, and the proofs are present to the mind without a new act of acquisition. This lesson unpacks that response.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector argues that any proposition that depends on a proof must be theoretical, not intuitive. Therefore, if the three propositions rely on the proofs given earlier, they cannot be intuitive.
This is a common misunderstanding.
2
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage from the matn and its translation. Notice the key terms: بديهة (intuition), نظرية (theoretical), ملحوظة (observed).
The passage is short but dense.
3
Analyze the answer
The author says the proofs are 'observed' (malḥūẓa) by rational people. He gives examples: 'If the whole were not greater than the part, the other part would have no effect' etc. These are implicit proofs that people use without being aware of them as formal proofs.
4
Grasp the key principle: العبرة بالمعنى لا بالعبارة
The consideration is by meaning, not by wording. Even if people don't phrase the proof exactly as the author did, they still have the meaning in mind.
This is a fundamental principle in Islamic epistemology.
5
Conclude the intuitive status
Since the proofs are already present to the mind (without new acquisition), the propositions remain intuitive. They are not dependent on a fresh act of reasoning.
This resolves the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما أمكن أن يقال : إن كل عاقل يعلم بالبديهة حقيقة هذه القضايا الثلاث وإن لم يخطر بباله تلك الحجج الدقيقة التي أوردتموها كيف ولو توقفت عليها لكانت نظرية غير بديهية؟ أشار إلى الجواب بقوله ( وهذه الاستدلالات) التي ذكرناها ( ملحوظة ) للعقلاء ) وإن عجز البعض عن تلخيصها في التعبير عنها ألا ترى إلى قولهم لو لم يكن الكل أعظم من الجزء لم يكن للجزء الآخر أثر البتة، ولو كان الشيء الواحد مساوياً لمختلفين لكان مخالفاً لنفسه فإنه إشارة إلى ما قررناه وإن لم تكن عبارته ملخصة كما لخصناها، لكن العبرة بالمعنى لا بالعبارة وليس يلزم من توقفها على هذه الحجج كونها نظرية لجواز كون الحجج ملحوظة بلا تجشم کسب جدید
And when it might be said that every rational being knows by intuition (al-badīha) the reality of these three propositions even if they do not occur to him those precise proofs (al-ḥijaj al-daqīqa) that you have adduced, how [can that be]? For if they depended on them, they would be theoretical (naẓariyya) not intuitive? He pointed to the answer by his saying: 'And these inferences (al-istidlālāt) that we have mentioned are observed (malḥūẓa) by rational people,' even if some are unable to summarize them in expression. Do you not see to their saying: 'If the whole were not greater than the part, the other part would have no effect at all; and if a single thing were equal to two different things, it would be contrary to itself.' For it is an indication of what we have established, even if its expression is not summarized as we have summarized it. But the consideration is by meaning (al-‘ibra bi-l-ma‘nā) not by wording (al-‘ibāra). And it does not follow from their dependence on these proofs that they are theoretical, because it is possible that the proofs are observed (malḥūẓa) without undertaking new acquisition.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector argues that if the three necessary propositions (the whole is greater than the part, things equal to a common thing are equal, a body cannot be in two places simultaneously) require the precise proofs that the author has given, then they would be theoretical (naẓariyya) rather than intuitive (badīhiyya), which contradicts the claim that they are known by intuition. The author's response, quoted from the matn, is that these inferences are 'observed' (malḥūẓa) by rational people even if some cannot articulate them. He gives examples: the proof that if the whole were not greater than the part the other part would have no effect, and if one thing were equal to two different things it would be self-contradictory. These are indications of what the author has established, even if not expressed in the same precise wording. The key point is that the meaning matters, not the wording. The dependence of these propositions on proofs does not make them theoretical because the proofs can be implicitly present to the mind without a new act of acquisition. Thus the intuitive status is preserved.

Key Terms
قضايا ثلاث
بديهة
حجج
نظرية
ملحوظة
العبرة بالمعنى لا بالعبارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary propositions cannot have any proofs; if they do, they become theoretical.
Correction: The author shows that necessary propositions can have proofs as long as those proofs are implicitly observed by the mind without a new act of acquisition.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do the three necessary propositions remain intuitive despite being supported by proofs?
A) Because the proofs are simple and everyone can articulate them.
B) Because the proofs are implicitly observed by the mind without new acquisition.
C) Because the prophets taught them directly.
D) Because they are based on sense perception.

Key Takeaway
The three necessary propositions remain intuitive because their proofs are implicitly present to the mind, and the essence matters more than the precise wording.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a belief because you couldn't articulate its proof in your own words, even though you intuitively knew it was true? What does this reveal about your reliance on expression over meaning?
?
The objector demanded explicit proofs to accept intuitive knowledge. In what area of your life do you demand perfect clarity from yourself before acting, and how does that delay your trust in what you already know?
?
When you accept a truth only when you can explain it elegantly, are you exalting your own articulation above the truth itself? How can you begin to value meaning over wording in your daily understanding of faith?
