فيلزم المحذور. وخلاصة الجواب أنه لا محذور لاختلاف الجهة، فإنه مثبت من حيث إنه من أفراد النظر ومثبت من حيث ذاته، هكذا ينبغي أن يحاط بمراتب الكلام. قوله: (استطراد إلخ) فيه بحث، لأنه لما ادعى الخصم أنه على تقدير أن يكون قولنا: النظر الصحيح مفيد للعلم نظرياً، يلزم إثبات الشيء بنفسه نظراً إلى أنه إثبات إفادة النظر بإفادة النظر، ولم يتعرض عند إقامة الشبهة بكلية الحكم، فكيف يكون ذكر المهملة في الجواب استطرادياً؟ بل يكون ذكر كل من الكلية والمهملة في الجواب لازماً قطعاً لمادة الشبهة. قوله: (لأن لزوم إلخ) فيه بحث، لأن منشأ اللزوم المذكور عدم ملاحظة خصوصية النظر المثبت، وهو مشترك بين الكلية والمهملة، بل في المهملة أظهر، لأنه يحتاج في الكلية إلى عدم ملاحظة الكلية في جانب المثبت أيضاً بخلاف المهملة. نعم لو كان منشأ اللزوم المذكور اندراج المثبت تحت المثبت على ما ذكره الشارح بقوله: فإن قلت إلخ، كان لزوم المحذور المذكور في الكلية دون المهملة، لكن ليس في عبارة المتن أثر من ذلك. وأما ما نقله من المحصل فلا ينفعه، لأن ذلك المذكور مبني على أن يكون المدعى جزئية كما اختاره الإمام، ولا شك أن اللازم حينئذ التسلسل أو الدور دون إثبات الشيء بنفسه، لأن الجزئية إذا أثبت بنظر جزئي آخر يكون إفادة ذلك النظر نظرياً، إذ لو كانت بديهية كانت الجزئية بديهية، فيحتاج إلى نظر جزئي آخر يكون إفادته أيضاً نظرية فيتسلسل أو يدور. قوله: (فلازمه الظاهر) أي معلوم الظهور، فالتعريف فيه من قبيل ووالدك العبد، وإيراد ضمير الفصل وتعريف المسند للدلالة على أن اللازم المعلوم الظهور مقصور على التسلسل لا يتجاوز إلى إثبات الشيء بنفسه، لا للإشارة إلى أن الدور لازم غير ظاهر، فإن لزوم الدور والتسلسل في مرتبة واحدة في اللزوم، ولما كان الدور مستلزماً للتسلسل استغنى بذكره عن ذكر الدور. قوله: (فلازمه الظاهر هو التسلسل) إنما قال: فلازمه الظاهر لاحتمال العود وأن يكون ذلك النظر داخلاً في المهملة وأن يكون عينها ولا تسلسل في شيء من الصور.
And know that the mention of the indefinite proposition in the investigation of the answer is a digression, because the necessary consequence of proving a thing by itself only appears in proving the universal by reasoning. As for proving the indefinite by reasoning, its apparent necessary consequence is regress. That is why he said in al-Muhassal: The judgment that reasoning may yield knowledge is theoretical, and regress is not necessary because it is permissible to end at a particular reasoning whose judgment of being knowledge-yielding is intuitive, such as our saying: a particular instance, such that that reasoning is included in the subject of that universal, then that particular reasoning would be proving its own judgment, so the problem arises. The summary of the answer is that there is no problem due to difference of aspect, for it is proven in so far as it is an individual of reasoning, and proven in so far as its essence. It is thus that one should comprehend the levels of discourse. He said: (digression etc.) There is discussion about it, because when the opponent claimed that on the supposition that our statement 'Correct reasoning yields knowledge' is theoretical, the proof of a thing by itself follows considering that it is a proof of the yielding of reasoning by the yielding of reasoning, and he did not address the universality of the judgment when presenting the doubt, so how is the mention of the indefinite in the answer a digression? Rather, the mention of both the universal and the indefinite in the answer is necessary to cut off the substance of the doubt. He said: (because the necessary consequence etc.) There is discussion about it, because the origin of the mentioned necessary consequence is the failure to observe the particularity of the proven reasoning, and it is common between the universal and the indefinite; indeed it is more apparent in the indefinite, because in the universal one also needs failure to observe universality on the side of the proof, unlike the indefinite. However, if the origin of the mentioned necessary consequence were the inclusion of the proven under the proof as the commentator mentioned in his statement 'if you say etc.', then the occurrence of the mentioned problem would be in the universal without the indefinite, but there is no trace of that in the wording of the text. As for what he transmitted from al-Muhassal, it does not help him, because that mentioned is based on the claim being particularity as chosen by the Imam (al-Razi), and there is no doubt that the necessary consequence then is regress or circularity, not proving a thing by itself, because if the particularity is proven by another particular reasoning, the yielding of that reasoning is theoretical, since if it were intuitive the particularity would be intuitive, so it needs another particular reasoning whose yielding is also theoretical, leading to regress or circularity. He said: (its apparent necessary consequence) i.e., known apparentness, so the definition in it is of the type 'and your father is the slave', and the use of the separating pronoun and the definition of the predicate to indicate that the apparent known necessary consequence is confined to regress and does not extend to proving a thing by itself, not to indicate that circularity is a non-apparent necessary consequence, for the occurrence of circularity and regress are on the same level in terms of necessity, and since circularity entails regress, mentioning it suffices over mentioning circularity. He said: (its apparent necessary consequence is regress) He only said 'its apparent necessary consequence' due to the possibility of return, and that that reasoning might be included in the indefinite, or be identical to it, and there is no regress in any of these cases.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage begins with a note that introducing the indefinite proposition (مهملة) in the answer is a digression (استطراد), because the charge of self-proof (إثبات الشيء بنفسه) only applies when proving a universal by reasoning, not an indefinite. For the indefinite, the danger is infinite regress (تسلسل). The author then references al-Muhassal (by al-Razi) to explain that regress can be avoided by ending at a particular reasoning whose judgment is intuitive. The passage then includes a detailed commentary analyzing why mentioning the indefinite is not a digression, arguing that the objection applies to both universal and indefinite. The discussion goes into the source of the necessity (لزوم) and whether it is based on the inclusion of the proven under the proof. The commentator concludes that the apparent necessary consequence for the indefinite is regress, not self-proof, and discusses the phrasing in the text regarding the 'apparent necessary consequence'.

Key Terms
مهملة (indefinite proposition)
استطراد (digression)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
دور (circularity)
إثبات الشيء بنفسه (self-proof)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the mention of the indefinite is truly a digression.
Correction: The commentary argues that it is necessary to address the doubt fully, so it is not a digression.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is the mention of the indefinite proposition not a digression?
A) Because the opponent's doubt does not specify universality, so both universal and indefinite must be addressed
B) Because the indefinite proposition leads to self-proof
C) Because it is mentioned in al-Muhassal
D) Because it avoids regress

Key Takeaway
The commentary shows that mentioning the indefinite is not a digression but essential, and clarifies that regress and self-proof are distinct problems.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study a difficult theological argument, do you ever dismiss a part as 'digression' because it seems irrelevant? After this lesson, can you identify a time when you might have done that? Will you commit to examining every element of a proof with equal seriousness, even when it is tedious?
?
The passage draws a precise distinction between regress and self-proof. Are you able to explain that distinction in your own words? If not, what specific step will you take to master this distinction—will you re-read the passage, discuss it with a teacher, or write a summary? Make a concrete commitment.
?
The commentator insists that mentioning the indefinite is necessary 'to cut off the substance of the doubt.' In your own pursuit of certainty in 'aqeedah, are you willing to engage with technical logical details for the sake of cutting off doubts? Or do you prefer to avoid such rigour? Sequential Attention Suffices for Inference
The commentator refutes the objection that both premises must be simultaneous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that sequential attention to premises, without a gap, is sufficient for inference, and why simultaneous intentional attention is not necessary.

Core Idea
Sequential attention to premises is enough; simultaneous focus is unnecessary.

This passage addresses a common objection: that inference requires both premises to be present in the mind at the same moment. The commentator argues that this is not necessary; it suffices for one premise to follow the other without a gap. The analogy of visual attention clarifies how this works.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening objection that inference requires both premises to be present together. Notice the phrasing 'اجتماع المقدمتين معاً'.
This is the common view being challenged.
2
Identify the upshot
Find the sentence beginning with 'والحاصل'. This gives the key point: simultaneous intentional attention is impossible, but sequential attention without a gap is possible and sufficient.
3
Study the analogy
The commentator provides an analogy with vision: looking at Zayd then Amr. Understand how the first person remains present even when you shift focus. Connect this to premises in inference.
This clarifies how two knowledges can coexist.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters for logical inference. It shows that the mind does not need to hold both premises in focal attention at the same moment; it can move sequentially.
This resolves the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن الشبهة بأنه لا يجب في الإنتاج اجتماع المقدمتين معاً، بل يكفيه حصول إحداهما عقيب الأخرى بلا فصل، إذ بذلك يتحقق النظر فيهما أعني الحركة المعدة لحصول النتيجة) والتوجه إلى مقدمة (غير العلم) بها (بل هو أي التوجه إليها هو النظر) فيها وملاحظتها قصداً) ولا يلزم من عدم اجتماع (النظرين) أي التوجهين إلى المقدمتين وملاحظتيهما القصديتين عدم اجتماع العلمين بالمقدمتين، والحاصل، إن التفات النفس إلى المقدمتين معاً دفعة بالقصد ممتنع، وأما حضورهما عند النفس بأن تلاحظ إحداهما قصداً، وتتوجه بالقصد إلى الأخرى عقيب الأولى بلا فصل، فيحضران معاً وإن لم تكونا ملحوظتين قصداً دفعة كطرفي الشرطية، فليس ممتنعاً وحضورهما على هذا الوجه هو المحتاج إليه في الإنتاج. الجواب أنك إذا حدقت نظرك إلى زيد وحده ثم حدقته كذلك إلى عمرو القائم وزيد مرئياً تبعاً لا قصداً في حال تحديقك إلى عمرو كان عمرو مرئياً قصداً، كذلك إذا لاحظت ببصيرتك مقدمة قصداً، وانتقلت منها سريعاً إلى ملاحظة مقدمة أخرى كذلك كانت الثانية ملحوظة قصداً، والأولى تبعاً فقد اجتمع العلمان وإن لم يجتمع التوجهان.
Concerning the objection that it is not necessary for inference that both premises be present together, rather it suffices for him that one of them obtains immediately after the other without separation, for thereby inquiry (al-nazar) into them is realized—namely the movement prepared for obtaining the result—and attention to a premise (not knowledge) of it, but rather it (i.e., attention to it is inquiry (al-nazar)) into it and observing it intentionally. And it does not follow from the absence of the two inquiries (i.e., the two attentions to the two premises and their intentional observations) the absence of the two knowledges of the two premises. The upshot: the mind's turning to both premises together at once intentionally is impossible. As for their presence in the mind such that one observes one intentionally and then intentionally turns to the other immediately after the first without separation, then they are present together even if they are not observed intentionally at once—like the two sides of a conditional—so it is not impossible, and their presence in this manner is what is needed for inference. The clarification: if you fix your gaze on Zayd alone, then fix it likewise on Amr who is standing, with Zayd being seen incidentally not intentionally while you are fixing your gaze on Amr, then Amr is seen intentionally; likewise, if you observe with your insight one premise intentionally, and then quickly move from it to observing another premise similarly, then the second is observed intentionally and the first incidentally, so the two knowledges come together even if the two attentions do not come together.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that for inference, both premises must be simultaneously present in the mind. The commentator argues that it is sufficient for one premise to follow the other without a gap. He explains that the mind cannot intentionally attend to both premises at the same moment. However, it can attend to one, then immediately shift attention to the other, so that both are present in the mind together, even if not both are the object of focused attention simultaneously. He provides an analogy: if you look directly at Zayd, then look directly at Amr while Zayd is still in your peripheral vision, both are present, but only one is directly attended to. Similarly, when considering two premises, after focusing on the first and moving quickly to the second, the first remains present in the mind as a background, and thus both are available for inference.

Key Terms
النظر
اجتماع المقدمتين
التوجه
الملاحظة القصدية
الإنتاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inference requires both premises to be consciously and simultaneously attended to in the mind at the same moment.
Correction: Sequential attention, where one premise immediately follows the other without a gap, is sufficient. The mind can hold the first premise incidentally while focusing on the second.

Checkpoint
What is the key claim made in this passage?
A) Both premises must be considered at the exact same moment.
B) Sequential attention to premises with no gap is sufficient for inference.
C) Inference is impossible without full focus on both premises.
D) The mind can attend to two things perfectly simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
Sequential attention to premises, without a gap, is sufficient for inference; simultaneous intentional focus is not required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever delayed a decision or judgment because you felt you didn't have perfect clarity on all premises at once, when sequential careful attention would have been enough?
?
When you seek knowledge, do you demand that every truth be fully present in your mind at all times, or can you trust that a truth stays with you incidentally while you attend to the next?
?
In your spiritual reflections, are you willing to move from one truth to another without a gap, or do you get stuck waiting for simultaneous illumination that may never come?

The Obligation is on Speculation, Not on Knowledge
Objection and answer on the obligation of speculative knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Amidi resolves the objection that obligating knowledge from speculation is impossible by distinguishing the obligation as falling on the act of speculation rather than its result.

Core Idea
The obligation is on the voluntary act of speculation, not on the involuntary knowledge that follows.

The passage presents a key objection against the doctrine that correct speculation leads to certain knowledge. The objector argues that obligating such knowledge is impossible because it is involuntary. The answer, attributed to al-Amidi, makes a crucial distinction: the obligation is not placed on the knowledge but on the speculation itself. This clarifies the nature of theological obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the passage and locate the objector's claim. Notice how he gives two arguments: one from consensus (knowledge of God is actual, so obligating it is impossible) and one from logical possibility (if not obligatory, speculation could fail to produce it).
The objector is not denying knowledge of God, but arguing about the nature of obligation.
2
Understand the answer
Find the response beginning with 'قلنا'. The answer states that the obligation is on the act of speculation, which is within human power, not on the resulting knowledge which follows necessarily.
The knowledge is described as 'واجب الحصول' (necessarily obtained) after speculation is performed.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why is this distinction important for theology? It shows that God's command is always within human capacity, even if the outcome is determined. This preserves both divine justice and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثالث : النظر الصحيح عند الجمهور ( وإنه ) أي قبح التكليف بالعلم الحاصل بعد النظر (خلاف (الإجماع لكونه واقعاً كما في معرفة الله سبحانه وتعالى ( أو لا يجب فيجوز) حينئذ (انفكاكه عنه ( عن النظر فلا تكون إفادته إياه مجزوماً بها ( وهو المطلوب ) عندنا (قلنا ) هو واجب الحصول بعده ( والتكليف ) إنما هو ( بالنظر ) المقدور لا بالعلم النظري الواجب الحصول كذا ذكره الآمدي
The Fifth Observatory - Third Objective: Correct speculation according to the majority. (And that it) i.e., the ugliness of obligating knowledge obtained after speculation (is contrary to) (consensus) because it is actual as in knowledge of Allah, may He be exalted. (Or it is not obligatory, so it may) then (be separated from it) i.e., from speculation, so its giving it is not certain. (And that is what is intended) for us. (We said:) It is obligatory to obtain after it. (And the obligation) is only (upon speculation) which is within power, not upon theoretical knowledge which must be obtained. Such is what al-Amidi mentioned.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer within the discussion of correct speculation. The objector argues that obligating knowledge that results from speculation is impossible because such knowledge is involuntary, as with necessary knowledge, and therefore it cannot be the object of obligation. He further claims that if it were not obligatory, then speculation could fail to produce it, so its production by speculation would not be certain. The answer is that the obligation is not placed on the resulting theoretical knowledge but on the act of speculation itself, which is within human power. Once speculation is performed, the knowledge necessarily follows, but the obligation attaches only to the voluntary act. This is attributed to al-Amidi.

Key Terms
تكليف
نظر
علم نظري
اضطراري
قدرة
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the objector is denying the possibility of knowing God.
Correction: The objector is not denying knowledge of God; rather, he is arguing about the nature of obligation—that obligating involuntary knowledge is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, what is the actual object of obligation in the case of knowledge from speculation?
A) The theoretical knowledge itself
B) The speculation that leads to it
C) Both the speculation and the knowledge
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The obligation in seeking knowledge of Allah is upon the act of speculation, which is within human capacity, not upon the resulting knowledge which follows necessarily.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek knowledge about Allah, do you focus more on achieving certainty or on the effort of thinking and reflecting? Which does this lesson say is truly within your control, and how will that change your approach starting today?

Think of a time you felt uncertain about a matter of faith. Did you stop speculating because you lacked clarity? Reflect on the duty to continue the act of speculation even when results are not immediate. What is one step you can take this week to engage in deeper reflection on a specific belief?
?
This lesson separates the act of seeking (speculation) from the resulting knowledge. Are you more concerned with the feeling of knowing or with the responsibility to seek? How can you prioritize the effort of pursuing truth over the comfort of having arrived?

The Connective 'Ba' in 'Bil-Nazar'
Commentator clarifies the preposition 'ba' in 'bil-nazar' as a connective, not causative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the preposition 'ba' in the phrase 'obligation by speculation' functions as a connective (sila) rather than a causative (sababiyya), and why this distinction matters for the scope of obligation.

Core Idea
The 'ba' in 'bil-nazar' connects, not causes — obligation is about the act within our power.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a subtle linguistic point with significant theological implications. He examines the preposition 'ba' in the phrase 'obligation by speculation' (al-taklif bi-l-nazar) and argues that it indicates connection, not causation. This distinction prevents a misinterpretation that would make knowledge indirectly obligatory through its speculative cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the key claim about 'ba'.
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Note the commentator's core argument that 'ba' is a connective (sila) not causative (sababiyya).
Pay attention to the phrase 'صلة التكليف وليست للسببية'.
2
Understand the difference between 'sila' and 'sababiyya'.
Contrast the two possible functions of 'ba'. If it is a connective, the obligation is 'concerning' or 'regarding' speculation. If causative, it would mean 'because of' speculation, implying causation.
The commentator explicitly rejects the causative interpretation.
3
See why the commentator rejects the causative interpretation.
The commentator argues that taking 'ba' as causative requires forced and laborious estimation. It would imply that obligation on knowledge is indirect, which is problematic.
He references Imam al-Razi's interpretation as an example of the causative reading.
4
Note how describing knowledge as 'obligatory to obtain' supports the analysis.
The description of knowledge as 'obligatory to obtain' indicates it is not the direct object of obligation because it is not within power, whereas speculation is described as 'within power' to show obligation applies directly to it.
This reinforces that the obligation is on the act of speculation, not on knowledge.
5
Recognize the implications for obligation being on actions within power.
The passage concludes that knowledge is not an action but a quality, relation, or affection, so it cannot be directly obligated. This aligns with the principle that obligation is only on actions within power.
This final point unifies the linguistic and theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا) بالعلم النظري إلخ أورد تتمة كلام المجيب ليتضح به أن الباء في قوله بالنظر صلة التكليف وليست للسببية فلا يمكن حمله على ما قاله الإمام بأن يقال : المعنى أن التكليف بالعلم بسبب النظر المقدور لنا فيكون مقدوراً لنا باعتبار التحصيل لأنه لا يمكن حمل الباء في قوله : لا بالعلم على السببية على أنه بعد حمل الباء على السببية استفادة ذلك المعنى منه، يحتاج إلى تعسف وتكلف تقدير، كما لا يخفي وفي توصيف العلم بقوله : الواجب الحصول إشارة إلى أن عدم التكليف به لعدم كونه مقدوراً كما أن توصيف النظر بالمقدور للإشارة إلى أن التكليف به لكونه مقدوراً ، لا لأن التكليف إنما هو بالأفعال والعلم ليس منها، فإنه خروج عن سوق الكلام كما لا يخفي . قوله: (وسيرد إلخ) حيث يقول : وتلخيصه أن المقدمة إذا كانت سبباً للواجب، أي مستلزماً بحيث يمتنع تخلفه عنه، فإيجابه إيجاب المقدمة في الحقيقة إذ القدرة لا تتعلق إلا بها إلى آخره . قوله : (لا) بالعلم النظري لأن التكليف إنما هو بالأفعال دون الكيفيات والإضافات والانفعالات والعلم لا يخرج من إحدى الثلاثة الأخيرة اتفاقاً .
His statement: '(Not) by theoretical knowledge etc.' He cited the completion of the respondent's speech to make clear that the 'ba' in his statement 'by speculation' is a connection (sila) of the obligation and not for causation. So it cannot be interpreted as the Imam said: 'The meaning is that the obligation of knowledge is because of speculation within our power, so it is within our power with regard to acquisition,' because the 'ba' in his statement 'not by knowledge' cannot be taken for causation; and after taking the 'ba' as causative, deriving that meaning from it requires forced and laborious estimation, as is not hidden. And in describing knowledge as 'obligatory to obtain' there is an indication that the absence of obligation upon it is due to its not being within power, just as describing speculation as 'within power' indicates that obligation upon it is because it is within power; not because obligation is only upon actions and knowledge is not among them, for that is a departure from the flow of discourse, as is not hidden. His statement: 'And it will come back to you etc.' where he says: 'Its summary is that if the premise is a cause for the obligatory, i.e., necessary such that its failure to occur is impossible, then its obligation is in reality an obligation of the premise, since power attaches only to it' up to the end. His statement: '(Not) by theoretical knowledge because obligation is only upon actions, not upon qualities, relations, and affections; and knowledge is not outside one of these three latter, unanimously.'
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic and conceptual analysis of the answer given earlier. The commentator explains that the preposition 'ba' in 'بالنظر' (by speculation) is a 'sila' (connection) of the act of obligation, not a 'sababiyya' (causation). This means that the obligation is 'regarding speculation' or 'concerning speculation', not 'because of speculation'. This distinction is important because if it were causative, the obligation would be on knowledge due to speculation being its cause, which would imply knowledge is within power indirectly. But the correct interpretation is that the obligation is directly on the act of speculation, which is within power. The description of knowledge as 'obligatory to obtain' indicates it is not the direct object of obligation due to its involuntariness. The commentator also dismisses the view that obligation is only on actions and knowledge is not an action, as a misreading. He then notes that a future discussion will summarize that if a premise is a necessary cause of an obligatory act, then the obligation is in truth on the premise. Finally, a supporting statement asserts that knowledge is not an action but a quality or affection, so it cannot be obligated directly.

Key Terms
باء
صلة
سببية
تكليف
مقدور
كيفيات
إضافات
انفعالات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The preposition 'ba' in 'bil-nazar' indicates causation (sababiyya).
Correction: The 'ba' is a connective (sila) between the obligation and the act of speculation, not a cause. Obligation is directly on speculation, not on knowledge via causation.

Checkpoint
What is the function of the preposition 'ba' in the phrase 'التكليف بالنظر' according to this passage?
A) It indicates causation (sababiyya)
B) It indicates connection (sila) of obligation
C) It indicates instrumentality
D) It indicates comparison

Key Takeaway
The preposition 'ba' in 'obligation by speculation' connects obligation to the act of speculation, not to its cause, making the directly obligated act the speculative act within our power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself feeling guilty for not possessing knowledge that you thought was obligatory, only to realize the obligation is actually on the effort of seeking, not the attainment? How does this lesson help you re-evaluate your spiritual responsibilities?
?
When you read the words 'obligatory to obtain' regarding knowledge, do you instinctively feel pressured to acquire it? How can you shift your focus from the outcome to the action within your power—the act of speculation itself?
?
In what areas of your life do you confuse causation with connection? Are there duties you assume are mandatory because of their results, rather than because of the direct act? How will this lesson change your approach to your daily obligations?

Clarifying 'Act' and 'Agent' in Tawlid and the Nature of Re-iterated Reflection
Commentary glossing the terms 'act' and 'agent' in tawlid, and explaining the Ash'arīs' use of 're-iterated reflection'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meanings of 'act' and 'agent' in the Ash'arī concept of tawlid, and why the Ash'arīs use the term 're-iterated reflection' to emphasize the irrelevance of time.

Core Idea
In tawlid, 'act' means the effect, not the act of affecting; time does not change the nature of reflection.

This lesson clarifies two key points from the commentary on the Ash'arī argument about tawlid. First, the term 'act' in the definition refers to the effect produced, not the act of producing, which avoids objections about knowledge not being an act. Second, the Ash'arīs call reflection 're-iterated' to stress that the only difference from initial reflection is temporal, and time has no bearing on the productive capacity of reflection. These clarifications are essential for understanding the structure of the Ash'arī proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on 'act' and 'agent'
Read the Arabic from 'قوله : أن يوجب فعل إلخ' to 'فيكونان متساويين في عدم التوليد' and the corresponding translation. Notice how the commentator distinguishes between 'effect' and 'affecting'.
The example of the hand and key illustrates generation of movement.
2
Identify the exclusion of the passive form
The phrase 'للفاعله' excludes cases like 'I broke it so it broke' where the second act is not for the same agent. Understand why this is important for the definition of tawlid.
3
Understand 're-iterated reflection'
The commentator explains that 're-iterated reflection' is identical to initial reflection except for the time of occurrence. Since time does not affect the logical structure, both are equal in not generating knowledge.
This counters objections that repetition somehow changes the nature of reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : أن يوجب فعل إلخ) المراد بالفعل في الموضعين الأثر لا التأثير بدليل تمثيلهم للتوليد بحركة اليد وحركة المفتاح، فلا يرد أن العلم ليس بفعل وكذا النظر ببعض التفسيرات . قوله : ( لفاعله) متعلق بيوجب واحترز به عن المطاوع نحو كسرته فانكسر، فإن فيه إيجاب فعل فعلاً آخر لكن ليس ذلك لفاعله. قوله : ( النظر المعاد إلخ المطابق لما سبق النظر المتذكر إلا أنه أورد لفظ المعاد ترويجاً للقياس بأنه هو النظر المبتدأ لا فرق بينهما إلا باعتبار الوقوع في الوقت الأول والثاني، ومن المعلوم أن الوقت لا دخل له فيكونان متساويين في عدم التوليد قوله : ( إذ لا فرق إلخ) لأن ما يعود إليه الاستلزام الصحة من حيث المادة والصورة، وهي متحدة فيهما . قوله: (ارتفاع التكليف بالمعارف (النظرية أي المعارف التي حصلت بالنظر يسقط قوله: (فعل لفاعله فعلاً آخر أراد بالفعل الأثر الحاصل من القادر أعم من أن يكون بواسطة أولا بها لا نفس التأثير فلا يرد أن العلم ليس من مقولة الفعل وكذا الحركة. قوله: (فقال الأصحاب إلخ) اعترض عليه بأن هذا لا يفيد اليقين لكونه عائداً إلى القياس الشرعي وسيشير إليه الشارح . قوله : (ارتفاع التكليف بالمعرف النظرية أي لا تبقى واجبة بمعنى أن لا يكون مأموراً بها، فلا يرد منع بطلان اللازم بناء على أن التكليف مقيد بعدم المعرفة، إذ تكليف العارف تكليف بتحصيل الحاصل، وذلك لأن معنى أن العارف لا يكلف أنه لا يتجدد له الأمر، والإيجاب لا أن معلومه يخرج عن كونه مأموراً به وعلى هذا يندفع أيضاً، ما يقال : من أن الارتفاع إنما يلزم إذا كانت المعارف النظرية كلها غير مقدورة لنا وغير حاصلة إلا بالتذكر، وأن قيد المعارف النظرية بالحاصلة من التذكر يمنع بطلان اللازم فتأمل . قوله: (قياس مركب القياس المركب قياس يستغني القياس فيه عن إثبات حكم الأصل بموافقة الخصم له مع أن الخصم يكون مانعاً لكون الحكم فيه معللاً بعلة المستدل إما بمنع عليتها أو بمنع وجودها فيه، والأول مركب الأصل والثاني مركب الوصف والتفصيل مذكور في كتب الأصول
His saying: 'that an act necessitates another act' etc. – what is meant by 'act' in both instances is the effect (al-athar), not the affecting (al-ta'thīr), as evidenced by their example of generation with the movement of the hand and the movement of the key. So it is not objected that knowledge is not an act, and similarly reflection according to some interpretations. His saying: 'for its agent' is connected to 'necessitates', and by it he excludes the passive form, like 'I broke it so it broke', for in that there is a necessitation of one act for another, but not for its agent. His saying: 're-iterated reflection' corresponds to previous 'remembered reflection', but he used the term 're-iterated' to promote the analogy, namely that it is the same as initial reflection; there is no difference between them except in regard to occurrence at the first and second time, and it is well-known that time has no influence, so they are equal in not generating. His saying: 'for there is no difference etc.' – because what pertains to the entailment is correctness in terms of matter and form, which are unified in both. His saying: 'the removal of obligation for theoretical cognitions' – i.e., the cognitions attained through reflection become non-obligatory... His saying: 'act for its agent another act' – he meant by 'act' the effect obtained from the agent, whether with or without an intermediary, not the affecting itself; so it is not objected that knowledge is not of the category of act, and similarly movement. His saying: 'so the Ashʿarīs said etc.' – it was objected that this does not yield certainty because it goes back to a juridical analogy (qiyās sharʿī), and the commentator will indicate that. His saying: 'the removal of obligation for theoretical cognitions' – i.e., they no longer remain obligatory in the sense that one is not commanded to acquire them, so it is not objected that the impossibility of the entailment is based on obligation being conditioned on the absence of knowledge, for obliging the knower is to oblige the attainment of what is already obtained. That is because the meaning of 'the knower is not obligated' is that no new command comes to him, not that what he knows exits from being commanded. And on this basis, what is said is also refuted: that the removal only follows if all theoretical cognitions are not within our power and are not obtained except through remembering, and that the qualification 'theoretical cognitions obtained from remembering' prevents the falsity of the entailment – so reflect. His saying: 'compound analogy' – a compound analogy (al-qiyās al-murakkab) is an analogy in which one dispenses with proving the ruling of the origin by the opponent's agreement, even though the opponent is preventing either by denying its cause (ʿilla) or by denying its existence in the origin. The first is compound in the origin (murakkab al-aṣl) and the second is compound in the description (murakkab al-waṣf), and the details are mentioned in the books of uṣūl.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from the sharh, glossing key terms and clarifying the structure of the Ashʿarī argument. It begins by explaining that 'act' in the definition of tawlid means the effect, not the act of affecting, which avoids objections that knowledge is not an act. It then clarifies the term 'for its agent' to exclude reflexive cases. The commentary explains that the Ashʿarīs used 're-iterated reflection' to stress that the only difference is temporal, which is irrelevant. It then discusses the objection that the Ashʿarī analogy is only a juridical analogy (qiyās sharʿī) and thus not certain. On page 251, the commentary addresses the removal of obligation: it means that theoretical cognitions are no longer commanded, not that they cease to be obligatory in a way that leads to impossibility. Finally, it defines the 'compound analogy' (qiyās murakkab) as one where the opponent already accepts the ruling in the origin but disputes its cause or its presence in the origin, distinguishing between compound in origin and compound in description.

أثر
تأثير
مطاوع
نظر معاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'act' in the definition includes the act of affecting itself, or that re-iterated reflection is different in kind from initial reflection.
Correction: The commentary clarifies that 'act' means the effect, and re-iterated reflection is identical to initial reflection except for the time of occurrence, which does not affect its nature.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why does the commentator say that the example of the hand and key shows that 'act' means effect, not affecting?
A) Because the hand's movement is the effect, not the affecting.
B) Because the hand's movement affects the key, so both are affecting.
C) Because the example shows generation without an intermediary.
D) Because the movement of the key is the effect of the hand's movement.

Key Takeaway
In tawlid, 'act' means the effect (athar), and re-iterated reflection is no different from initial reflection except in time, so it cannot generate knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often repeat the same theological reasoning hoping for certainty. This lesson says time does not change the nature of reflection. What does that reveal about your reliance on human reasoning? Will you seek knowledge through revelation and guidance instead of endless repetition?
?
The passage distinguishes between the effect (athar) and the act of affecting (ta'thir). In your daily worship, do you focus more on the actions themselves or the effects they have on your heart and conduct? Which one did Allah command you to perfect? How will you shift your focus starting today?
?
If re-iterated reflection is no different from initial reflection, why have you continued to trust your repeated arguments over the clear texts? What step will you take to submit to the Quran and Sunnah rather than your own intellect?

Removal of Obligation for Theoretical Cognitions and the Definition of Compound Analogy
Commentary on the removal of obligation and the definition of compound analogy with its subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arī position that theoretical cognitions are not obligatory after attainment, and the definition of a compound analogy (qiyas murakkab) with its two types.

Core Idea
Once theoretical cognitions are attained, obligation is removed; compound analogy uses opponent's acceptance of the origin's ruling.

This lesson covers two related topics from the commentary. First, the Ash'arīs argue that once theoretical cognitions are obtained through reflection, the obligation to acquire them is lifted. This refutes the objection that it leads to obliging the attainment of what is already obtained. Second, the commentator defines a 'compound analogy' (qiyas murakkab) as one where the opponent already accepts the ruling in the origin but disputes either the cause (illa) or its presence in the origin. Understanding this dialectical structure is key to the Ash'arī method.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on removal of obligation
Read from 'قوله: (ارتفاع التكليف بالمعارف (النظرية' to 'والنظرية هي المعارف الحاصلة بالتذكر' and the translation. Notice how the commentator explains that 'removal' means no new command, not that the knowledge ceases to be obligatory.
This addresses an objection: if the knower is not obligated, it might imply impossibility, but the commentator clarifies.
2
Understand the objection and response
The objection is that if obligation is removed, it would mean that theoretical cognitions are not obligatory at all, but the Ash'arīs respond that it only means no new command for the knower.
The qualification 'theoretical cognitions from remembering' is crucial.
3
Study the definition of compound analogy
Read the Arabic from 'قوله: (قياس مركب' to the end. The commentator defines it as an analogy where you don't need to prove the origin's ruling because the opponent accepts it, but disputes its cause or presence in the origin.
The two types: compound in origin (murakkab al-asl) and compound in description (murakkab al-wasf).

?

?

The Authority of Consensus: Responding to the Objection from Individual Error
Al-Jurjani responds to an objection about consensus being a proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani refutes the objection that individual error implies collective error, thereby defending the definitive authority of consensus.

Core Idea
The whole is not the sum of its parts in matters of error.

This passage tackles a familiar objection: if every scholar can err, how can their collective agreement be infallible? Al-Jurjani's answer is a masterclass in logical distinctions, separating individual possibility from collective impossibility. He also grounds his argument in religious necessity, showing that consensus is a definitive proof known directly from the religion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage (up to 'قلنا') which contains the objector's argument. Notice how it claims that error can spread from one individual to all.
2
Identify Al-Jurjani's first response
After 'قلنا', the author states that consensus is a definitive proof known necessarily from religion. Understand why doubting such necessary knowledge is considered sophistry.
This response appeals to religious necessity, not logical argument.
3
Understand the distinction
The second response distinguishes between permissibility of error for each individual and for the whole collective. The house analogy illustrates this: each person can fit in the house, but not all together.
The key term here is 'al-kull al-majmūʿī' (the collective whole).
4
Consider the final point
The author concludes that the possibility of cumulative error is repelled by necessary religious knowledge and proofs of community infallibility. Reflect on how this strengthens the authority of consensus.
This shows that the answer is both rational and based on revealed sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصادر من أحدهم على انفراده ) إلى الخطأ) الصادر من واحد آخر وهكذا إلى أن يشملهم الخطأ بأسرهم ( لا يوجب الصواب) بل يوجب كون الكل على الخطأ ( قلنا : ( كون الإجماع حجة قطعية ( معلوم بالضرورة من الدين) فيكون التشكيك فيه بالاستدلال في مقابلة الضرورة سفسطة لا يلتفت إليها ) ولا يلزم من جواز الخطأ على كل واحد جواز الخطأ على الكل ( المجموعي ( لتغايرهما وتغاير حكميهما ) فإن كل واحد من الإنسان تسعه هذه الدار ولا تسع كلهم وأما احتمال انضمام الخطأ إلى الخطأ حتى يعم الكل فمدفوع بما علم من الدين ضرورة، وبما ثبت بالأدلة من عصمة الأمة
From one of them individually to error from another, and so on until error encompasses all of them, it does not necessitate correctness, but rather necessitates that all are in error. We said: the fact that consensus is a definitive proof is known necessarily from religion, so doubting it by reasoning in opposition to necessity is sophistry not to be heeded. And the permissibility of error for each individual does not necessitate permissibility of error for the whole collectively, because they are different and their rulings are different. For each individual of mankind may fit in this house, but not all of them. As for the possibility of error being added to error until it encompasses the whole, it is repelled by what is known necessarily from religion, and by what is established by proofs of the infallibility of the community.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer regarding the authority of consensus (ijmāʿ). The objector argues that since each individual can err, the whole community can also err, so consensus does not guarantee truth. The answer distinguishes between error applying to each individual (kull wāḥid) and error applying to the collective whole (al-kull al-majmūʿī). The author uses an analogy: each individual can fit in a house, but not all together, showing that the property of individuals does not transfer to the collective. The possibility of accumulation of errors is rejected by the necessity of religion and the established proofs of the infallibility of the community. The author concludes that consensus is a definitive proof (ḥujja qaṭʿiyya) known necessarily from religion, so rejecting it is sophistry.

Key Terms
إجماع
حجة قطعية
ضرورة
خطأ
كل مجموعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If each individual can err, then the whole collective must also be capable of error.
Correction: Al-Jurjani argues that the permissibility of error for individuals does not entail permissibility for the collective whole, because they have different rulings.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the permissibility of error for the whole community compared to individuals?
A) It necessarily follows from the permissibility of error for each individual.
B) It does not follow, because the whole is different from its parts.
C) It is impossible because the community is infallible.
D) It is possible but not certain.

Key Takeaway
The objection that individual error implies collective error is refuted by distinguishing between individual parts and the collective whole, and by affirming that consensus is a definitive proof known necessarily from religion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your own intellect leads you to doubt something you know is necessarily from the religion—like the authority of consensus—do you stop and label that doubt as sophistry, or do you entertain it? What does your answer reveal about your submission to divine guidance?
?
Think of a time you preferred your own reasoning over the collective wisdom of the scholars. Did that path bring you closer to certainty or to confusion? Are you willing today to trust the community's infallibility even when you cannot see the logic?
?
If Al-Jurjani's argument is true—that collective error does not follow from individual error—then what excuse do you have for not seeking knowledge from the consensus? Will you commit to learning what is necessarily known from the religion, so that you can recognize sophistry when it appears?

Impossibility of Obligation Without Prerequisite
Al-Sharh explains the objection and answer concerning obliging a thing without its prerequisite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between obliging a thing without its prerequisite and obliging a thing along with commanding the omission of its prerequisite, and why the latter is the true impossibility.

Core Idea
The real impossibility is not the lack of prerequisite obligation, but commanding existence and non-existence together.

In this passage, al-Sharh clarifies a subtle point in the logic of obligation. A common misconception is that obliging something without its prerequisite is always impossible. The author explains that the true impossibility arises only when one is commanded to do a thing and simultaneously commanded to omit its prerequisite.

Guided Path
1
Read the Opening Objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is presented: if something is absolutely obligatory, it must remain obligatory even when its prerequisite is absent.
Notice the structure of the argument.
2
Follow the Dialectical Exchange
Trace the 'fa-in qulta' and 'qultu' sections to see the back-and-forth between objector and author.
Identify the key moves.
3
Understand the Verification
Focus on the phrase 'al-tahqeeq' where the author identifies the true impossible: commanding both the thing and the omission of its prerequisite.
This is the core distinction.
4
Consider the Alternative Interpretation
Reflect on the final section about carrying the wording, which offers a subtle rephrasing to avoid ambiguity.
This shows scholarly precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ذلك الشيء ( عليه لزم تكليف المحال ( لبقاء الوجوب حال عدم الموقوف عليه، وإلا لم يكن وجوباً مطلقاً ) وهو ضعيف إذ المحال أن يجب الشيء مع عدم المقدمة لا عدم التكليف بها فإن عدم التكليف بها لا يستلزم عدمها كما أن التكليف بها لا يستلزم وجودها بل كل من وجودها وعدمها يجامع كلاً من إيجابها وعدم إيجابها فإن قلت إذا لم تكن المقدمة واجبة جاز له تركها فإذا تركها فإن لم يبق وجوب الواجب لم يكن واجباً مطلقاً، وإن بقي فقد وجب الشيء مع عدم المقدمة قلت هذا بعينه جاز فيما إذا تركها مع كونها واجبة والتحقيق أن المحال هو أن يكلف بالشيء مع التكليف بعدم مقدمته معه لا مع عدم التكليف بمقدمته، ولك أن تحمل عبارة الكتاب على هذا بأن تقول تقديرها إذ المحال أن يجب وجوب الشيء مع عدم المقدمة
قوله : ( وإلا أي إن لم يبق الوجوب حال عدم الموقوف عليه لم يكن الوجوب وجوباً مطلقاً لكونه حينئذ واجباً على تقدير وجود الموقوف عليه . قوله : ( أن يجب الشيء مع عدم المقدمة لأنه طلب لوجود الشيء حال عدمه. قوله : ( لا يستلزم عدمها ) فيجوز أن يجامع التكليف بالشيء بوجود المقدمة . قوله : (فإن قلت إلخ إثبات للزوم التكليف بالمحال بأنه على تقدير عدم وجو المقدمة يلزم ما اعترفت بكونه محالاً، وهو وجوب الشيء مع عدم المقدمة . قوله : قلت هذا بعينه إلخ ) اكتفى بالنقض ولم يورد الحل لظهوره، وهو أن المحال وجود الشيء مع عدم مقدمته لا وجوبه معه
تحصيله
قوله : ( والتحقيق) أي التحقيق في بيان ضعف قد يجاب .
قوله : ( هو أن يكلف بالشيء إلخ ) فإنه تكليف لوجود الشيء وعدمه. قوله : (لا مع عدم التكليف بمقدمته) فإن عدم التكليف بها لا يستلزم عدمها .
قوله : قلت هذا بعينه جار فيما إذا تركها مع كونها واجبة قيل فيه بحث لأن المقدمات إذا دخلت في الإيجاب بحيث يكون إيجاب الواجب إيجاباً لها أيضاً، كان معنى وجوبه مع مقدماته كوجوب الصلاة على الجنب والمحدث، فقولنا : بوجوبه عند عدم مقدماته قول بوجوب تحصيلها، أما إذا لم تجب المقدمات فقولنا : بوجوبه عند عدم المقدمات تكليف مع بالمحال والفرق دقيق يظهر بالإمعان فليتأمل . قوله : ( ولك أن تحمل عبارة الكتاب إلخ قد يقال : لا حاجة إلى تقدير الوجود في تصحيح عبارة الكتاب لحصوله : بأن يجعل مع عدم المقدمة ظرفاً لغواً متعلقاً بيجب، فيكون المعنى إذ المحال أن يجب الشيء ويجب عدم المقدمة كما في قولك، وخرج زيد مع عمرو، وأنت خبير بأن قوله لا مع عدم التكليف بها لا يدفع هذا التوجيه إذ المعنى حينئذ ليس المحال أن يجب الشيء، ويجب عدم التكليف بالمقدمة ولا تقريب به هاهنا . وتجعل لفظة متعلقة بالوجود المقدر، فتدبر
Regarding that thing, it would necessitate obliging the impossible (because the obligation remains in the state of absence of what it depends on, otherwise it would not be an absolute obligation). But it is weak, because the impossible is that a thing be obligatory while its prerequisite is absent, not that it be obligatory while the prerequisite is not made obligatory, for the non-obligation of the prerequisite does not necessitate its non-existence, just as its obligation does not necessitate its existence. Rather, both its existence and non-existence can combine with both its being obligatory and not obligatory. If you say: when the prerequisite is not obligatory, he is allowed to omit it; if he omits it, then if the obligation does not remain, the obligatory is not absolutely obligatory; if it remains, then the thing has become obligatory without the prerequisite. I say: this very same thing applies when he omits it while it is obligatory. The verification is that the impossible is that one be obligated with the thing along with the obligation to omit its prerequisite, not that one be obligated without the obligation of its prerequisite. You may interpret the wording of the book in this way by saying its implied meaning is that the impossible is that the obligation of a thing should exist along with the absence of its prerequisite.
His statement: (Otherwise, if the obligation does not remain in the state of absence of what it depends on, then the obligation would not be absolute, because it would then be obligatory only on the condition of the existence of what it depends on.) His statement: (That a thing be obligatory without the prerequisite) because it is a demand for the existence of a thing in a state of its absence. His statement: (does not necessitate its non-existence) so it is permissible that the obligation of the thing can coexist with the existence of its prerequisite. His statement: (If you say, etc.) is a proof of the necessity of obliging the impossible, because on the assumption of the non-existence of the prerequisite, what you have admitted to be impossible follows, namely the obligation of the thing without the prerequisite. His statement: (I say this very same, etc.) He sufficed with the rebuttal and did not present the solution because it is obvious, which is that the impossible is the existence of a thing without its prerequisite, not its obligation along with it.
His statement: (And the verification) i.e., the verification in explaining a weakness that might be answered.
His statement: (Is that one is obligated with the thing, etc.) for it is an obligation to both the existence and non-existence of the thing. His statement: (not with the non-obligation of its prerequisite) because the non-obligation does not necessitate its non-existence.
His statement: (I say this very same applies when he omits it while it is obligatory.) It was said there is a discussion in it because if the prerequisites are included in the obligation such that the obligation of the obligatory is also an obligation for them, then the meaning of its obligation along with its prerequisites is like the obligation of prayer for the junub and the hadith. So our saying that it is obligatory while its prerequisites are absent is a statement of the obligation to obtain them. But if the prerequisites are not obligatory, then our saying that it is obligatory while the prerequisites are absent is an obligation of the impossible, and the difference is subtle and appears upon reflection, so let it be considered. His statement: (And you may interpret the wording of the book, etc.) It may be said: there is no need to assume existence in correcting the wording of the book, because it is obtained by making 'ma'a 'adam al-muqaddima' an extra adverb attached to 'yajib', so the meaning becomes: the impossible is that a thing should be obligatory and the absence of the prerequisite should be obligatory, as in your saying 'and Zayd went out with Amr'. And you are aware that his statement 'not with the non-obligation of its prerequisite' does not repel this interpretation, because the meaning then is not that the impossible is that a thing be obligatory and the non-obligation of the prerequisite be obligatory, and there is no approximation here. And you make the word 'with' attached to the assumed existence, so reflect.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of an objection and its answer concerning the impossibility (al-muhaal) of obliging a thing without its prerequisite (al-muqaddima). The argument begins by stating the opponent's claim: if something is obligatory absolutely, then it must remain obligatory even when its prerequisite is absent, otherwise it wouldn't be absolute. The author says this is weak. The real impossibility is that the thing itself be obligatory while the prerequisite is absent, not that the prerequisite be not obligatory. He explains that making the prerequisite obligatory or not does not affect its existence. Then an objection (fa-in qulta) is raised: if the prerequisite is not obligatory, one may omit it; if he does, either the obligation remains (which would be obliging the impossible) or it doesn't (which would mean the obligation wasn't absolute). The answer (qultu) is that the same problem occurs even if the prerequisite were obligatory. The verificaion (al-tahqeeq) is that the true impossible is to command both the thing and the negation of its prerequisite simultaneously, not to command the thing without commanding the prerequisite. The commentary then explains each part of the matn, clarifies terms, and provides an alternative interpretation regarding the phrasing. The passage concludes by urging reflection on the subtle difference.

Key Terms
تكليف المحال
المقدمة
الوجوب المطلق
الموقوف عليه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Obliging a thing without its prerequisite is always impossible.
Correction: The true impossibility is obliging a thing along with commanding the omission of its prerequisite. Obliging without commanding the prerequisite is not necessarily impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the true 'impossible' (al-muhaal) in terms of obligation?
A) That a thing be obligatory while its prerequisite is absent.
B) That a person be obligated with the thing along with an obligation to omit its prerequisite.
C) That the prerequisite be not obligatory.
D) That the obligation of the thing ceases when its prerequisite is absent.

Key Takeaway
The true impossible in obligation is commanding both the existence of a thing and the non-existence of its prerequisite simultaneously.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself unable to fulfill an obligation, do you tend to blame the absence of some prerequisite as the reason? How does this lesson challenge you to examine whether you are simply avoiding the command to act?
?
Have you ever convinced yourself that a duty is impossible because you felt a prerequisite was missing, while in reality the real barrier was your unwillingness to commit both to the duty and to securing its prerequisite? What would it look like to stop making that excuse today?
?
This lesson forces a razor-sharp distinction between the absence of a prerequisite and the command to omit it. In your own life, where might you be conflating the two—believing God's command is impossible when in fact He has not commanded you to omit the prerequisite? How will you repent from that misjudgment?

The Nuance of 'Ma'a' in Conditional Phrases
The author explains the grammatical/logical nuance of the word 'مع' in conditional phrases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the word 'ma'a' can either include the non-existence of the antecedent under the obligation or become a condition on the obligation, depending on whether existence is assumed.

Core Idea
'Ma'a' can change from including non-existence to conditioning obligation based on the assumption of existence.

In Arabic logical and grammatical analysis, the word 'ma'a' (with) plays a subtle role in conditional phrases. The author of this passage highlights a key distinction: when existence is assumed, 'ma'a' makes both existence and non-existence part of the obligation; when not, it becomes a condition. This short text unpacks that nuance, warning against false analogies from everyday usage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two functions of 'ma'a'
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the author introduces two possibilities: 'ma'a' as an adverbial (ظرف) either essential (مستقر) or non-essential (لغو). The key is whether we assume existence.
The term 'ظرف لغو' refers to an adverbial that is not essential to the sentence structure.
2
Understand the consequence of assuming existence
When existence is assumed, 'ma'a' makes both the thing's existence and the non-existence of its antecedent obligatory. Consider the logical difference when the antecedent's non-existence is required.
This ties back to the original text's discussion of conditional obligations.
3
Contrast with the case where existence is not assumed
When existence is not assumed, 'ma'a' becomes a condition (قيد) on the obligation, not part of the obligated scope. Thus only the existence of the thing in the time of conjunction is required, not the non-existence of the antecedent.
This is the subtle point the commentator urges reflection upon.
4
Consider the everyday example: 'Zayd went out with Amr'
The author shows that this example is not analogous because the implication of Amr's going out comes from external context, not from 'ma'a' itself.
This highlights the danger of false analogies in logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وتجعل لفظة مع إلخ) أي ظرفاً له مستقراً أو لغواً، فإن مع إذا أضيف إلى أحد المتصاحبين يكون ظرف زمان أو مكان فيكون وجود الشيء وعدمه داخلين تحت الوجوب، فيفيد وجوب وجود الشيء ووجوب عدم مقدمته بخلاف ما إذا لم يقدر الوجود، سواء جعل لفظة مع ظرفاً مستقراً أو لغواً، فحينئذ يكون قيداً للوجوب لا داخلاً تحته، فيفيد وجوب الشيء في زمان مقارنته لعدم المقدمة لا وجوب عدم المقدمة، ولهذه الدقيقة أمر الشارح بالتدبر، وأما نحو قولنا : خرج زيد مع عمرو فإفادته خروج عمرو باعتبار أمر خارج عن مدلول اللفظ، فإنه إذا كان خروج زيد في زمان اجتماعه ومصاحبته لعمرو، يلزم أن يكون عمرو أيضاً خارجاً فقياس ما نحن فيه عليه خروج عن التحقيق.
The statement: 'And you make the word ma'a etc.' meaning it is an adverbial (ẓarf) either essential (mustaqarr) or non-essential (laghw). For when ma'a is appended to one of two mutual associates, it becomes an adverbial of time or place, so the existence of the thing and its non-existence become included under the obligation (al-wujūb). Thus it entails the obligation of the existence of the thing and the obligation of the non-existence of its antecedent, contrary to the case where existence is not assumed, whether we make the word ma'a an essential adverbial or a non-essential one. In that case, it becomes a condition (qayd) on the obligation, not included under it, so it entails the obligation of the thing in the time of its conjunction with the non-existence of the antecedent, not the obligation of the non-existence of the antecedent. It is for this subtle point that the commentator ordered reflection. As for our saying: 'Zayd went out with Amr', its entailment of Amr's going out is based on an external matter beyond the meaning of the word. For if Zayd's going out is in the time of his meeting and accompanying Amr, it necessarily follows that Amr also went out. So applying an analogy from that to our case is a departure from achieving the truth.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical/logical explanation of the phrase 'وتجعل لفظة مع' from the original text. The author clarifies that the word 'مع' can function as an adverbial (ظرف) either as a stable adverbial (ظرف مستقر) or a non-stable adverbial (ظرف لغو). When attached to one of two mutually associated things, it indicates a time or place relationship. The key nuance is whether the existence of the thing is assumed or not. If existence is assumed, then both the existence of the thing and the non-existence of its antecedent are included under the obligation. But if existence is not assumed ('if we do not assume existence'), then 'مع' becomes a condition (قيد) on the obligation rather than part of the obligated scope. This subtlety leads the commentator to instruct reflection (تدبر). Then the author contrasts with the everyday example 'Zayd went out with Amr' to show that its implication of Amr's going out is due to external context, not the linguistic meaning itself. So one cannot analogize that to the case at hand.

Key Terms
ظرف مستقر
ظرف لغو
مع
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'مع' always makes the associated thing obligatory.
Correction: It depends on whether existence is assumed. Without assuming existence, 'مع' becomes a condition on the obligation, not making the associated thing's existence obligatory.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if we do not assume existence when using 'ma'a' in a conditional phrase?
A) It includes non-existence under the obligation.
B) It makes 'ma'a' a condition on the obligation.
C) It removes the obligation entirely.
D) It changes the tense.

Key Takeaway
The word 'ma'a' can either include the non-existence of the antecedent under the obligation or become a condition on the obligation, depending on whether the existence of the thing is assumed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Material Fallacy Does Not Necessarily Cause Ignorance
The author argues that material fallacy only sometimes causes ignorance, not absolutely, and distinguishes matter corruption from form corruption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why material fallacy (fasad al-madda) does not always lead to ignorance and how a valid logical form can yield true conclusions even from false premises.

Core Idea
Material fallacy can yield truth if the form is valid; only form corruption blocks knowledge.

In this passage, the author examines a common assumption: that any argument with false premises must lead to ignorance. He argues that this is not always true, because the logical form of the argument matters. Even with false premises, if the form is valid, a true conclusion is possible. This insight helps refine our understanding of how reasoning works.

Guided Path
1
Read and understand the claim
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the author states that material fallacy does not necessarily cause ignorance; it may do so in some forms but not absolutely.
The key phrase is: 'فالصواب أن الفاسد من جهة المادة قد يستلزم الجهل في بعض الصور، وأما استلزامه إياه مطلقاً فلا'.
2
Analyze the example
Look at the example: 'Zayd is a donkey; every donkey is a body; therefore Zayd is a body'. Despite the false premise, the conclusion is true. This shows that material fallacy does not always cause ignorance.
The example is given to refute the idea that material fallacy necessitates ignorance absolutely.
3
Distinguish matter vs. form
Understand the distinction: corruption can be from matter (false premises) or from form (invalid logic). Only form corruption prevents any belief from arising. If the form is valid, even false premises can yield a true conclusion.
The passage states: 'وإلا ... فلا يستلزم النظر الجهل'.
4
Explore the commentary on the objection
The commentary addresses an objection: the conclusion 'Zayd is a donkey-body' is ignorance. But the author explains that a further inference from that yields a true statement, so the original inference does not necessarily cause ignorance.
This shows the subtlety: the initial conclusion may be false, but it can be used in a valid syllogism to produce truth.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Summarize why material fallacy does not absolutely cause ignorance. The key is the role of logical form. Only when the form is corrupted does inference fail to produce any belief.
Remember: truth can emerge from falsehood if the logical structure is sound.

وثالثها أن الفساد إن كان من المادة فقط (استلزمه لما مر من استدلال الإمام وفيه بحث لأن قولنا : زيد حمار أن زيداً وكل حمار جسم ينتج وليس بجهل فالصواب أن الفاسد من جهة المادة قد يستلزم الجهل في بعض الصور، وأما استلزامه إياه مطلقاً فلا كيف وقد بين في الميزان كيفية استنتاج الصادق من المقدمات الكاذبة ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن من جهة المادة فقط، بل كان من الصورة فقط أو منهما معا ( فلا) يستلزم النظر الجهل ( إذ الضروب الغير المنتجة وهي التي فسدت صورتها سواء كانت مقدماتها صادقة أو كاذبة ) لا تستلزم اعتقاداً أصلاً لا خطأ ولا صواباً . قوله: (استلزمه) أي مطرداً في جميع المواد، وقد عرفت أن الحق عدم الاستلزام في شيء من الصور بما لا مزيد عليه . قوله : ( وليس بجهل أجاب عنه الشارح في حواشي شرح التجريد بأن اللازم زيد جسم حماري، وهو جهل وفيه أنه لو ضم هذه النتيجة إلى قولنا وكل جسم حماري فهو جسم ينتج زيد جسم مع أن كلا النظرين فاسدان من جهة المادة، فالجواب عنه ما يستفاد مما قاله أن النظر المذكور لا يستلزم العلم في نفس الأمر لعدم تحققه فيه لكون الصغرى كاذبة بل على تقدير صدقه فيه ولا نزاع فيه . قوله: (المقصد التاسع كان الظاهر ذكره متصلاً بالمقصد الخامس المشتمل على قوله : ( وثالثها أن الفساد إن كان من المادة فقط استلزمه الظاهر أن المراد هو الاستلزام الكلي وعليه مدار البحث وأنت خبير بأن فساد المادة قد يكون بالكذب، وقد يكون بعدم المناسبة على ما تقرر في الميزان والفاسد بالمعنى الثاني لا يستلزم الجهل، بل قد يفيد العلم وهو ظاهر ، فكأنه أراد بفساد المادة القسم الأول فقط . قوله : ( وفيه بحث لأن قولنا إلخ قد يجاب عن البحث بأن النتيجة هي أن زيداً جسم حماري، وهو كاذب قطعاً كذا في حاشيته للتجريد، واعترض عليه بأن ثبوت الجسم الحماري يستلزم ثبوت مطلق الجسم، فيصدق في الجملة وليس بشيء، فإن الصدق في الجملة على الوجه المذكور لا ينافي استلزامه الجهل بالنظر إلى تمام النتيجة، فيندفع البحث حينئذ وليس مقصود المجيب إلا ذاك
And the third is that if the corruption is only from the material (fasad al-madda), it necessitates [ignorance] due to what has passed of the proof of the Imam; but there is discussion because our statement 'Zayd is a donkey' — Zayd and every donkey is a body yields a conclusion, and it is not ignorance. So the correct view is that the materialy corrupt may necessitate ignorance in some forms; as for its necessitating it absolutely, no, for it has been explained in al-Mizan how true conclusions are derived from false premises. And if not (i.e., if the corruption is not from the material only but from the form only or from both), then inference does not necessitate ignorance, because the non-productive moods – those whose form is corrupted, whether their premises are true or false – do not necessitate any belief at all, neither error nor correctness. His saying: 'it necessitates it' means universally in all matters, and you have known that the truth is no necessitation in any form without addition. His saying: 'and it is not ignorance' – the commentator answered in his marginal notes on Sharh al-Tajrid that the necessary result is 'Zayd is a donkey-body', and that is ignorance; and there is that if this conclusion is added to our statement 'every donkey-body is a body', it yields 'Zayd is a body', even though both inferences are materially corrupt. The answer is understood from what he said: that the said inference does not necessitate knowledge in itself because it is not realized in it, as the minor premise is false, but even assuming its truth, there is no dispute. His saying 'the ninth maqsad – it would have been apparent that it should be mentioned connected to the fifth maqsad' includes his saying 'and the third that if the corruption is from the material only, it necessitates it'; apparently what is meant is universal necessitation, and upon that revolves the investigation. You are aware that material corruption may be due to falsehood or lack of suitability as established in al-Mizan. The corrupt in the second sense does not necessitate ignorance; rather it may yield knowledge, and that is apparent. So it is as if he intended by material corruption only the first division. His saying 'and there is discussion because our statement etc.' – the discussion may be answered by saying the conclusion is that Zayd is a donkey-body, and it is definitely false, as in his marginalia on al-Tajrid. It was objected that the existence of donkey-body necessitates absolute body, so it is true in some respect; but that is not valid, because truth in some respect in the mentioned manner does not negate its necessitating ignorance with respect to the complete conclusion. Thus the discussion is repelled, and the objective of the answerer is nothing but that.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the third point concerning whether material fallacy (fasad al-madda) necessarily leads to ignorance. The author argues that material fallacy does not always cause ignorance; it may do so in some cases but not universally. He gives the example of the syllogism 'Zayd is a donkey; every donkey is a body; therefore Zayd is a body' – even though the minor premise is false, the conclusion is true, so ignorance does not necessarily result. He distinguishes between corruption of matter (fasad al-madda) and corruption of form (fasad al-sura). Only when the form is valid can a true conclusion be drawn from false premises. The commentary then addresses a specific objection: that the conclusion 'Zayd is a donkey-body' is ignorance. The answer is that while that particular conclusion is false, a further inference from it yields a true conclusion (Zayd is a body), so the initial inference does not necessarily cause ignorance. The author also clarifies that material corruption can be due to falsehood or lack of suitability; only the first type may cause ignorance. The passage concludes by defending the view that material fallacy does not necessitate ignorance absolutely, and a specific objection is resolved by noting that the conclusion in question, when taken completely, is false but does not prevent the possibility of true knowledge from other inferences.

فساد الصورة
استلزام الجهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any argument with false premises (material fallacy) necessarily leads to ignorance.
Correction: The passage shows that if the logical form is valid, false premises can yield a true conclusion, so material fallacy does not always cause ignorance.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does material fallacy (fasad al-madda) necessarily cause?
A) Ignorance in all cases
B) Ignorance in some cases but not absolutely
C) Knowledge in all cases
D) No belief at all

Key Takeaway
Material fallacy does not always cause ignorance; when the logical form is valid, true conclusions can be drawn from false premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you were certain a conclusion was false simply because you disagreed with the premises. How does this lesson challenge your instinct to dismiss an entire argument based on its starting points? Will you now examine the logical form before rejecting it?
?
This lesson warns that formal corruption (invalid reasoning) yields no belief at all. Are there areas in your own thinking where you have accepted conclusions based on weak or invalid logic, simply because the premises seemed true? What will you do to guard against this?
?
The author says that even from false premises, truth can emerge if the form is sound. In your spiritual life, have you ever learned a true lesson from a flawed teacher or imperfect source? How does this shape your willingness to seek truth wherever it may be found, while still holding the source accountable?

The Role of Awareness in Reasoning
Author defines التفطن and defends Ibn Sina's view against al-Baydawi's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why awareness of the logical form (التفطن) is necessary for reasoning to yield knowledge and how the debate between Ibn Sina and al-Baydawi clarifies what this awareness entails.

Core Idea
Awareness of the logical form is the key that unlocks knowledge from premises.

This passage delves into the epistemology of reasoning. It argues that simply having premises is not enough; one must be aware of how these premises lead to the conclusion. This awareness, called التفطن, is a conceptual act that distinguishes valid reasoning from mere possession of data. The author presents and critiques an objection from al-Baydawi, ultimately clarifying that the necessary awareness is of the inclusion of the minor under the middle, not an extra mental act beyond proper arrangement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Focus on the flow: the initial claim, the objection, the response, and the final clarification. Then read the translation to ensure understanding.
Notice the dense logical terminology; don't get bogged down by every detail on the first read.
2
Identify the main claim and the objection
Locate the statement that reasoning requires awareness of the aspect (الجهة) that makes premises lead to the conclusion. Then find the objection by al-Baydawi based on the difference in clarity among logical figures.
The objection is meant to prove that awareness of the form is a condition because two figures with equally evident premises can yield different clarity in conclusions.
3
Analyze the author's critique
The author responds by pointing out that the difference in clarity may be due to differences in the implications (اللوازم) or implied things, not necessarily the form itself. Understand the argument about figures and implications.
The author suggests that the variation in clarity could stem from the specific logical relationships involved, not just the shape of the argument.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
The author endorses Ibn Sina's view: the required awareness is the conjunction of premises in the mind with proper arrangement and the observation of the inclusion of the minor under the middle. This is not an extra condition beyond proper arrangement.
Key distinction: arrangement itself is necessary, but observing that arrangement is not a separate condition beyond having the premises appropriately structured in mind.
